1 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Pattison v. State


No. 27S05-1603-CR-115


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


54 N.E.3d 361; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 440



June 22, 2016, Decided 
June 22, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Grant County Superior Court, No. 27D03-1303-FD-110. The Honorable Warren Haas, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 27A05-1411-CR-517.
Pattison v. State, 2015 Ind. App. LEXIS 735, 47 N.E.3d 621 (Ind. Ct. App., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Jurors would reasonably interpret the instruction to mean they must presume defendant's ACE while driving was more than 0.08 if the State proved his ACE was more than 0.08 within three hours of stopping him, but defendant could offer evidence leading them to reject that finding; [2]-The State was still required to prove the ACE of the defendant within three hours of the stop; [3]-The arresting officer must first have probable cause to believe the defendant had an unlawful ACE behind the wheel before detaining that individual to be tested.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

3 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Gibson v. State


No. 22S00-1206-DP-00360


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


51 N.E.3d 204; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 264



April 12, 2016, Decided 
April 12, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Gibson v. State, 2016 Ind. LEXIS 479 (Ind., June 29, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gibson v. Indiana, 137 S. Ct. 54, 196 L. Ed. 2d 56, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 5015 (U.S., 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gibson v. Ind., 2017 U.S. LEXIS 1417 (U.S., Feb. 21, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Floyd Superior Court 1, No. 22D01-1205-MR-1145. The Honorable Susan L. Orth, Judge.
Gibson v. State, 2015 Ind. LEXIS 828, 43 N.E.3d 231 (Ind., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-There was sufficient evidence to prove that defendant murdered the victim while committing criminal deviate conduct under Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(b)(1)(D) (2008 Repl.) because his own statements indicated the victim was alive when he sexually assaulted her; [2]-The trial court did not err under Ind. Code § 35-34-1-5(c) in allowing the State to amend the charging information to change the allegation that defendant "committed" another murder to that he had been "convicted" of it; defendant had sufficient notice of the original aggravator and the proposed amendment, had an opportunity to object, and then had an opportunity to defend against it; [3]-The death sentence was not inappropriate in light of the nature of the offense; upon overpowering and strangling the victim, he bit and chewed her labia and inserted his whole fist in her vagina, breaking her back in the process.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

5 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Whistle Stop Inn, Inc. v. City of Indianapolis


No. 49S02-1604-MI-175


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


51 N.E.3d 195; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 242



April 11, 2016, Decided 
April 11, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D14-1310-MI-38464. The Honorable James B. Osborn, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1407-MI-519.
Whistle Stop Inn, Inc. v. City of Indianapolis, 2015 Ind. App. LEXIS 480, 36 N.E.3d 1118 (Ind. Ct. App., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-An ordinance that banned smoking in bars and taverns but exempted businesses licensed as satellite gambling facilities did not violate Indiana's Equal Privileges and Immunities Clause, Ind. Const. art. I, § 23, because the exemption was reasonably related to the inherent differences distinguishing other types of businesses from satellite gambling facilities, which had to comply with state requirements that included describing their smoke removal equipment pursuant to Ind. Code § 4-31-5.5-4(11) in the licensing application; [2]-The ordinance did not create a monopoly or treat similarly situated classes disparately because its requirements were not arbitrary, but were tailored to the type of business, and any intent to gain economic benefits was not improper because the ordinance had non-economic health purposes and the exemption was not shown to be a privilege for purchase.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

6 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Buncich


No. 45S00-1409-PL-587


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


51 N.E.3d 136; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 209



March 22, 2016, Decided 
March 22, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Lake County Circuit Court, No. 45C01-1407-PL-84. The Honorable George C. Paras, Judge. On Direct Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Ind. Code § 3-11-1.5-3.4 (Supp. 2015), which creates a Small Precinct Committee in Lake County and directs it to identify precincts with fewer than 500 active voters that may be amenable to consolidation, is not unconstitutional because Lake County's proportion and number of small precincts is sufficiently exceptional to justify the special application of § 3-11-1.5-3.4 under Ind. Const. art. IV, § 23, and because the precinct committee persons are not state officers under Ind. Code § 3-5-2-17 within the ambit of the separation of powers doctrine, Ind. Const. art. III, § 1.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

7 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Myers v. Crouse-Hinds Div. of Cooper Indus.


No. 49S00-1502-MI-119, No. 49S00-1501-MI-35, No. 49S00-1501-MI-36


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


53 N.E.3d 1160; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 156



March 2, 2016, Decided 
March 2, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Myers v. Crouse-Hinds Div. of Cooper Indus., 2016 Ind. LEXIS 311, 53 N.E.3d 1173 (Ind., 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-1405-MI-14372. The Honorable Timothy W. Oakes, Judge. Interlocutory Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-9801-MI-1-360. The Honorable Theodore M. Sosin, Judge. Interlocutory Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-9801-MI-1-360. The Honorable Theodore M. Sosin, Judge.
Myers v. Crouse-Hinds Div. of Cooper Indus., 2015 Ind. LEXIS 177, 26 N.E.3d 613 (Ind., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Ind. Code § 34-20-3-2 violated Ind. Const. art. 1, § 23 because the disparately treated classes were identically comprised of asbestos victims and the statute did not differentiate between them based on any single characteristic of theirs, inherent or otherwise; whether asbestos victims were seeking relief from defendants who mined and sold raw asbestos or from defendants who provided products containing asbestos did not constitute an inherent distinguishing difference between the asbestos victims; [2]-Because plaintiffs alleged injuries caused by a disease that might have been contracted as a result of protracted exposure to a foreign substance, the Indiana Product Liability Act's statute of repose provision, Ind. Code § 34-20-3-1, did not apply to bar their claims for asbestos injury and illness.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.

 

8 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Zerbe


No. 49S05-1509-MI-529


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


50 N.E.3d 368; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 140



February 25, 2016, Decided 
February 25, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion County Superior Court, No. 49D13-1403-MI-9780. The Honorable Timothy W. Oakes, Judge.

On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A05-1410-MI-463.
State v. Zerbe, 2015 Ind. LEXIS 754, 37 N.E.3d 493 (Ind., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in granting appellee's petition for removal from the Indiana sex offender registry because although the amended definition of "sex offender" in the Indiana Sex Offender Registration Act, Ind. Code §§ 11-8-8-7(a), 11-8-8-19(f) (Supp. 2015), included a person who was required to register as a sex offender in any jurisdiction resulted in the affirmative obligation to notify another state government, the amended definition was non-punitive as applied to appellee, and as such, did not offend the Ex Post Facto Clause of the Indiana Constitution, Ind. Const. art. I, § 24; because appellee was required to register as a sex offender in Michigan, maintaining that requirement in Indiana did not retroactively punish him.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

Tyson v. State


No. 45S03-1509-CR-528


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


51 N.E.3d 88; 2016 Ind. LEXIS 141



February 25, 2016, Decided 
February 25, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Lake County Superior Court, No. 45G01-1301-FD-11. The Honorable Salvador Vasquez, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 45A03-1405-CR-143.
Tyson v. State, 28 N.E.3d 1074, 2015 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 216 (Ind. Ct. App., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Denial of the motion to dismiss the charge of failure to register as a sex offender was proper because the petitioner was statutorily obligated to register in Indiana under Ind. Code § 11-8-8-5 since he had to register as a sex offender in another state; maintaining that obligation across state lines was far from excessive, especially relative to the significant public safety purpose the sex offender registry served.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Thornton v. State


No. 49S02-1512-PL-709


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


43 N.E.3d 585; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 1067



December 22, 2015, Decided 
December 22, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D11-1402-PL-003833. The Honorable John F. Hanley, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1409-PL-662.
Thornton v. State, 38 N.E.3d 1013, 2015 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 953 (Ind. Ct. App., 2015)

11 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Comm'r of the Ind. BMV in His Official Capacity v. Vawter


No. 49S00-1407-PL-494


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


45 N.E.3d 1200; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 926



November 6, 2015, Decided 
November 6, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Vawter v. Abernathy, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 3271 (U.S., May 16, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D14-1305-PL-06159. The Honorable James B. Osborn, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Indiana's personalized license plates (PLPs) are government speech because the Indiana Bureau of Motor Vehicles (BMV) has final approval authority by Ind. Code §§ 9-13-2-125 and 9-18-15-4, and exercises effective control; thus, the BMV did not violate U.S. Const. amends. I or XIV in denying an application for a PLP or revoking a previously issued PLP; [2]-Procedural due process protections did not apply because vehicle owners did not have a property interest in their PLPs; without due process protections, applicants whose PLPs were denied or revoked had no entitlement to reasons for the BMV's decision or to a hearing where they could present evidence.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded for an award of summary judgment in favor of the BMV.

 

12 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Tiplick v. State


No. 49S04-1505-CR-287


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


43 N.E.3d 1259; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 852



October 7, 2015, Decided 
October 7, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49G20-1210-FC-70439. The Honorable Steven R. Eichholtz, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A04-1312-CR-617.
Tiplick v. State, 2015 Ind. App. LEXIS 37, 25 N.E.3d 190 (Ind. Ct. App., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The synthetic drug statute, Ind. Code § 35-31.5-2-321 (2012), was not vague because "synthetic drug" was defined in § 35-31.5-2-321, the statute named the Ind. Code § 25-26-13-4.1 emergency rules as the only additional source for prohibited substances, and § 25-26-13-4.1(c) described where to look for the published rules, based on the procedures contained in Ind. Code § 4-22-2-37.1; the three statutes gave clear guidance as to how to find everything falling within the definition of "synthetic drug" under § 35-31.5-2-321; [2]-The charging information was inadequate under Jennings because the only synthetic drug listed in the information or probable cause affidavit was XLR11; XLR11 was only illegal at the time pursuant to the Indiana Board of Pharmacy's Emergency Rule 12-493(E), and neither the charging information nor the probable cause affidavit referenced the Rule.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, dismissed in part, and remanded.

 

13 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Layman v. State


No. 20S04-1509-CR-548


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


42 N.E.3d 972; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 792



September 18, 2015, Decided 
September 18, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Laman v. State, 2015 Ind. LEXIS 1001 (Ind., Dec. 2, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Elkhart Circuit Court. No. 20C01-1210-MR-7, 20C01-1210-MR-5. The Honorable Terry C. Shewmaker, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 20A04-1310-CR-518.
Layman v. State, 2014 Ind. App. LEXIS 458, 17 N.E.3d 957 (Ind. Ct. App., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-While the evidence was sufficient to sustain defendants' burglary convictions, the evidence was not sufficient to sustain a conviction for felony murder in the perpetration of a burglary, in violation of Ind. Code § 35-42-1-1(2) (2012), because when the group broke into and entered the residence of the homeowner intending to commit a theft, not only were they unarmed, but also neither defendants nor their cohorts engaged in any dangerously violent and threatening conduct; there was simply nothing about defendants' conduct or the conduct of their cohorts that was clearly the mediate or immediate cause of their friend's death.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

14 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


B.B. v. B.C. (In re I.B.)


No. 82S05-1502-AD-63


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


32 N.E.3d 1164; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 510



June 11, 2015, Decided 
June 11, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Vanderburgh Superior Court, Nos. 82D07-1302-AD-22 and 82D07-1302-AD-23. The Honorable Renée Allen Ferguson, Magistrate, The Honorable Brett J. Niemeier, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 82A05-1402-AD-65.
B.B. v. B.C. (In re I.B.), 2014 Ind. App. LEXIS 522, 19 N.E.3d 784 (Ind. Ct. App., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-While, under the circumstances of the case, Ind. Code § 31-19-11-1(c) (2008) barred an adoption by the maternal grandmother and her fiance that, to all appearances, would otherwise be in the children's best interest, that did not make the statute unconstitutional as applied because its prohibitions against adoptions by convicted felons were rationally related to a legitimate legislative purpose and did not discriminate against a suspect class; hence, it was necessary to remand the case for reconsideration of both adoption petitions.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

Isom v. State


No. 45S00-0803-DP-125


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


31 N.E.3d 469; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 431



May 20, 2015, Decided 
May 20, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected May 20, 2015.
Rehearing denied by Isom v. State, 2015 Ind. LEXIS 673 (Ind., July 28, 2015)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Isom v. Indiana, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 1205 (U.S., Feb. 29, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Lake Superior Court, No. 45G04-0708-MR-00008. The Honorable Thomas P. Stefaniak, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant failed to show the court erred in failing to grant his for-cause challenges of certain prospective jurors; [2]-Declarants' identification of defendant as the shooter was a nontestimonial statement and the court did not violate defendant's constitutional rights by allowing it at trial; [3]-The court did not abuse its discretion in declining to give a voluntary manslaughter instruction to the jury because there was no evidentiary dispute regarding sudden heat; [4]-Prosecutor's remarks were not so prejudicial to defendant's right of fundamental due process as to make a fair trial impossible; [5]-The court exceeded its statutory authority by ordering the death sentences to be served consecutively.


OUTCOME: Conviction and sentence affirmed, and case remanded.

 

16 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Hoagland v. Franklin Twp. Cmty. Sch. Corp.


No. 49S02-1410-PL-643


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


27 N.E.3d 737; 2015 Ind. LEXIS 233



March 24, 2015, Decided 
March 24, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion County Circuit Court, No. 49D02-1111-PL-42082. The Honorable Theodore M. Sosin, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1301-PL-44.
Hoagland v. Franklin Twp. Cmty. Sch. Corp., 2014 Ind. App. LEXIS 255, 10 N.E.3d 1034 (Ind. Ct. App., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Summary judgment in favor of the school corporation was proper because the corporation did not violate a constitutional mandate by discontinuing transportation services to and from school for the majority of its public school students since Ind. Const. art. 8, § 1, did not mandate school corporations to provide transportation to and from school; the Indiana Legislature made policy decisions regarding exactly what qualified as a part of a uniform system of public education, and that would not be disrupted unless there was a clear violation of the Indiana Constitution.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

17 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Morgan v. State


No. 49S02-1405-CR-00325


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


22 N.E.3d 570; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 968



December 18, 2014, Decided 
December 18, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49F18-1208-FD-060696. The Honorable Reuben B. Hill, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1304-CR-00386.
Morgan v. State, 2014 Ind. App. LEXIS 51, 4 N.E.3d 751 (Ind. Ct. App., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because defendant was provided sufficient notice of the type of conduct that was prohibited, and neither arbitrary nor discriminatory enforcement would be authorized or encouraged, the supreme court upheld the constitutionality of the public intoxication statute, Ind. Code § 7.1-5-1-3 (Supp. 2014), by reading a reasonableness standard into "annoys"; [2]-An objective reasonableness standard was used in many areas of the law as an appropriate determinant of liability and a guide to conduct, and it provided a constraining and intelligible enforcement guideline for police and prosecutors; [3]-The evidence was insufficient to sustain defendant's conviction for public intoxication because defendant was asleep at a bus shelter, which was unlikely to annoy a reasonable person; [4]-There were no other people at the bus shelter aside from defendant, his brother, and a police officer.


OUTCOME: Conviction vacated.

 

18 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


First Am. Title Ins. Co. v. Robertson


No. 49S04-1311-PL-732


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


19 N.E.3d 757; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 904



November 13, 2014, Decided 
November 13, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified by, On rehearing at First Am. Title Ins. Co. v. Robertson, 2015 Ind. LEXIS 236 (Ind., Mar. 26, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court No. 7, No. 49D07-1105-PL-019374. The Honorable Michael D. Keele, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A04-1206-PL-326.
First Am. Title Ins. Co. v. Robertson, 990 N.E.2d 9, 2013 Ind. App. LEXIS 223 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in failing to grant the motion of the Commissioner of the Indiana Department of Insurance to dismiss an insurer's petition for judicial review because the insurer did not file the agency record with the trial court pursuant to the Administrative Orders and Procedures Act (AOPA), Ind. Code § 4-21.5-5-13, and thus, its petition could not be considered; [2]-A petitioner for review could not receive consideration of its petition where the statutorily-defined agency record, AOPA, Ind. Code § 4-21.5-3-33(b), had not been filed, and that bright-line approach best served the goals of accuracy, efficiency, and judicial economy.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

Zoeller v. Sweeney


No. 45S00-1309-PL-596


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


19 N.E.3d 749; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 893; 201 L.R.R.M. 3376; 165 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,535



November 6, 2014, Decided 
November 6, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Lake Superior Court, Cause No. 45D01-1305-PL-52. The Honorable John M. Sedia, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Ind. Const. art. I, § 21 required just compensation when the state demanded particular services, not when the federal government did so; [2]-The union's federal obligation to represent all employees in a bargaining unit was optional and occurred only when the union elected to be the exclusive bargaining agent, for which it was justly compensated by the right to bargain exclusively with the employer.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

20 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Ind. Dep't of State Revenue v. Caterpillar, Inc.


No. 49S10-1402-TA-79


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


15 N.E.3d 579; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 652



August 25, 2014, Decided 
August 25, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Indiana Department of State Revenue Letter of Findings, No. 08-0333R. On Petition for Review from the Indiana Tax Court, No. 49T10-0812-TA-70. The Honorable Martha Blood Wentworth, Judge.
Caterpillar, Inc. v. Ind. Dep't of State Revenue, 988 N.E.2d 1269, 2013 Ind. Tax LEXIS 4 (Ind. Tax Ct., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A corporate taxpayer could not deduct foreign source dividends when calculating Indiana net operating losses (NOLs) because the three-step process outlined in Ind. Code § 6-3-2-2.6(c) for calculating NOLs did not incorporate a foreign source dividend deduction when determining federal NOLs, when modifying federal NOLs under Ind. Code § 6-3-1-3.5(b), or when allocating and apportioning modified federal NOLs under Ind. Code §§ 6-3-2-2, 6-3-2-2.6(d)(2); [2]-The reference in § 6-3-2-2.6(c) to the modifications required by § 6-3-1-3.5 encompassed only § 6-3-1-3.5 and did not incorporate by reference every modification to Indiana adjusted gross income (AGI) found anywhere in the Indiana tax statutes; [3]-No Commerce Clause violation resulted from disallowing the foreign source dividend deduction in the Indiana NOL calculation because the deduction was allowed in calculating AGI.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

21 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Gaddie v. State


No. 49S02-1312-CR-789


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


10 N.E.3d 1249; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 516



June 27, 2014, Decided 
June 27, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49F19-1208-CM-53729. The Honorable Shatrese M. Flowers, Master Commissioner.

On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1212-CR-953.
Gaddie v. State, 991 N.E.2d 137, 2013 Ind. App. LEXIS 324 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The evidence was insufficient to convict defendant of resisting law enforcement by fleeing a police order to stop, because the disturbance and the officer's presence did not provide sufficient evidence to prove the element that the order to stop was supported by probable cause or reasonable suspicion; the offense of resisting law enforcement by fleeing after being ordered to stop required that an officer's order to stop be based on probable cause or reasonable suspicion of criminal activity.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the trial court reversed.

 

22 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Knapp v. State


No. 28S00-1305-LW-327


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


9 N.E.3d 1274; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 485



June 12, 2014, Decided 
June 12, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Knapp v. Ind., 2015 U.S. LEXIS 330 (U.S., Jan. 12, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Greene Circuit Court, No. 28C01-1108-MR-1. The Honorable Erik A. Allen, Judge. On Direct Appeal from a Sentence of Life Imprisonment Without Parole.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Sufficient evidence showed defendant was on probation, as an aggravator under Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(a) and (b)(9)(C) because the State showed he was on probation for a felony when the victim was murdered; [2]-The State did not ask the jury to consider non-statutory aggravators, contrary to Ind. Const. art. 1, § 16, because it only said his probation for drugs was linked to a drug-induced murder, he had an "uncaring attitude" toward probation, not the victim, and an unsupported comment was fleeting; [3]-Life without parole for murder while on non-reporting probation for the lowest-level felony did not violate Ind. Const. art. 1, § 16 or the Eighth Amendment because the crime was the gravest offense known to Indiana law, it was a particularly flagrant abuse of probation, and the crime he was on probation for involved the drug that induced the murder.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

23 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. State


No. 48S02-1406-CR-363


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


10 N.E.3d 1; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 449



June 2, 2014, Decided 
June 2, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Madison Circuit Court, No.48C01-1012-MR-00783. The Honorable David A. Happe, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 48A02-1212-CR-1007.
Brown v. State, 994 N.E.2d 761, 2013 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 954 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the 150-year aggregate sentence imposed on defendant, a juvenile offender tried as an adult under Ind. Code § 31-30-104(a) and convicted of two murders and a robbery, was appropriate. HOLDINGS: [1]-Under the authority of Ind. App. R. 7(B), revision of the sentence was appropriate because defendant was guilty of murder under a theory of accomplice liability, the crime was not particularly heinous, his drug use from childhood reduced his culpability for the path that led him to the crime, and consideration should have been made for the fact that defendant was only sixteen years old at the time of the crime.


OUTCOME: Convictions affirmed. Case remanded for issuance of new sentencing order.

 

24 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Fuller v. State


No. 48S02-1406-CR-364


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA

David v. Kleckner


No. 49S02-1405-MI-355


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


9 N.E.3d 147; 2014 Ind. LEXIS 444



May 28, 2014, Decided 
May 28, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D13-1208-MI-30944. The Honorable Timothy W. Oakes, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1301-MI-13.
David v. Kleckner, 989 N.E.2d 843, 2013 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 725 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a wrongful death medical malpractice case brought by the deceased patient's husband as administrator of her estate was barred by the applicable statute of limitations in Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b), because the malpractice action was commenced more than two years after the actual occurrence of the alleged act of medical negligence. HOLDINGS: [1]-The health care provider was not entitled to summary judgment on his defense asserting the medical malpractice statute of limitations because the deceased patient's husband established an issue of fact material to a theory that avoided the defense. There was not necessarily an unreasonable delay before the action was commenced and the husband may have filed within a reasonable time if the trigger date occurred within the statutory window.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment reversed.

Brewington v. State

SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA

May 1, 2014, Decided 
May 1, 2014, Filed

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Brewington v. Ind., 2015 U.S. LEXIS 435 (U.S., Jan. 12, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Dearborn Superior Court II, No. 15D02-1103-FD-84. The Honorable Brian Hill, Special Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 15A01-1110-CR-550.
Brewington v. State, 981 N.E.2d 585, 2013 Ind. App. LEXIS 14 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court of appeals erred in its free-speech analysis by failing to distinguish between defendant's attacks on his victims' reputations that were protected by the stringent actual malice standard, and his true threats to their safety, in violation of Ind. Code § 35-45-2-1(c)(6) and (7) (2008), that received no such protection; [2]-Defendant's actions towards the judge constituted a "true threat" beyond the scope of free speech protection; any lingering doubt as to whether defendant's threat was worth taking seriously was erased when defendant publicized the judge's home address; [3]-A reasonable person similarly situated to the doctor would have been amply justified in perceiving defendant's behavior as a threat to physical safety because defendant announced that he knew where the doctor lived and publicized a private family photograph of the doctor.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.

M & M Inv. Group, LLC v. Ahlemeyer Farms, Inc.

September 26, 2013, Decided 
September 26, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Bartholomew Circuit Court, No. 03C01-1109-CC-5167. The Honorable Stephen R. Heimann, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 03A04-1112-CC-639.
M & M Inv. Group, LLC v. Ahlemeyer Farms, Inc., 972 N.E.2d 889, 2012 Ind. App. LEXIS 332 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A U.S. Supreme Court decision did not abrogate an Indiana Supreme Court decision on required notice of a tax sale because the U.S. Supreme Court did not address notice to a mortgagee, each case turned on its facts, and the Indiana General Assembly did not amend Ind. OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

Becker v. State


No. 45S03-1301-CR-9


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


992 N.E.2d 697; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 632



August 22, 2013, Decided 
August 22, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Lake Superior Court, No. 45G04-9702-CF-37. The Honorable Thomas P. Stefaniak, Jr., Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 45A03-1112-CR-539.
Becker v. State, 969 N.E.2d 140, 2012 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 709 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: whether the Indiana Department of Corrections (DOC) could contest an adverse sex-offender registration ruling which a local prosecutor did not appeal. HOLDINGS: [1]-the DOC could not contest the earlier ruling because it was bound by that ruling under principles of res judicata, as the DOC and the local prosecutor, as representatives of the State with the same substantial interest in keeping defendant on the sex-offender registry for as long as possible, were in privity, for res judicata purposes, and the local prosecutor more than adequately represented that interest; [2]-the DOC could not contest the ruling because, when it intervened in the case in which the adverse ruling had been issued, it was bound by that ruling, which had long since become binding, so its intervention came too late to contest the ruling.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

36 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Fry v. State


No. 09S00-1205-CR-361


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


990 N.E.2d 429; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 475



June 25, 2013, Decided 
June 25, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by Fry v. State, 2015 Ind. App. LEXIS 68, 25 N.E.3d 237 (Ind. Ct. App., Feb. 4, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Cass Superior Court, No. 09D02-1109-MR-2. The Honorable Rick Maughmer, Judge. On Direct Appeal Pursuant to Indiana Appellate Rule 4(A).
Fry v. Schroder, 986 N.E.2d 821, 2013 Ind. App. LEXIS 206 (Ind. Ct. App., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with murder in the Cass Superior Court (Indiana). He sought bail and a declaration that Ind. Code § 35-33-8-2(b), giving him the burden to prove he should be granted bail, was unconstitutional. The trial court ordered the State to show evident proof, or a strong presumption, under Ind. Const. art. 1, § 17, that defendant committed murder, and denied him bail, while holding the statute unconstitutional. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of the constitutionality of Ind. Code § 35-33-8-2(b) and the denial of his bail request. The supreme court found the statute unconstitutional because (1) the State, seeking to apply the exception to bail in Ind. Const. art. 1, § 17, had to prove it, (2) making defendant prove his innocence to receive bail violated his presumption of innocence, (3) the State was better able to present its evidence, instead of making defendant prove the evidence did not exist, (4) placing the burden on defendant made him, while incarcerated, disprove the State's case pre-trial, and (5) a grand jury indictment was insufficient to deny bail, so the State had the burden. This showing had to be made by a preponderance of the evidence because (1) "probable cause" made the exception in Ind. Const. art. 1, § 17 have no meaning, (2) the "beyond a reasonable doubt" standard made the State wait until its entire case was prepared before arresting a defendant, and (3) the preponderance standard was sufficient given the severity of the crime and the attendant consequences. It was not an abuse of discretion to deny defendant bail because the evidence supported this result.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

37 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Berry v. Crawford


No. 49S00-1201-PL-53, No. 49S00-1202-PL-76


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


990 N.E.2d 410; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 465



June 18, 2013, Decided 
June 18, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Berry v. Crawford, 2013 Ind. LEXIS 789 (Ind., Oct. 8, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Consolidated Appeals from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D10-1106-PL-23491. The Honorable David J. Dreyer, Judge. On Transfer Pursuant to Indiana Appellate Rule 56(A).
Berry v. Crawford, 963 N.E.2d 1117, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 292 (Ind., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the State of Indiana, the Indiana Auditor of State, and the Speaker and Clerk of the Indiana House of Representatives, sought review of a judgment from the Marion Superior Court (Indiana), which ordered the return of amounts withheld from the per diem pay of appellee legislators and issued a permanent injunction preventing future withholding.


OVERVIEW: On two occasions, some legislators absented themselves to prevent the formation of a quorum. The House imposed fines on the absent legislators. The legislators asserted various statutory and constitutional challenges to the withholding of the fines. The court held that the legislators' claims were nonjusticiable based on separation of powers under Ind. Const. art. III, § 1. The constitutional grant of jurisdiction in Ind. Const. art. IV, §§ 10, 11, 14 to each house of the legislature over its internal proceedings and the discipline of members was exclusive. Although the courts had jurisdiction under Ind. Const. art. VII, §§ 1, 4, 6, 8 to review the case in the first instance, the discipline issues involved were matters of legislative power under Ind. Const. art. IV, § 1. The constitutional grant of legislative power to compel attendance and to punish members was not limited by either the right to protest in Ind. Const. art. IV, § 26 or the compensation requirements of Ind. Const. art. IV, § 29. Legislators who had been absent were not entitled to compensation for services not performed, and they had no property right upon which a takings or due process claim could be predicated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded with directions to dismiss for lack of justiciability.

 

38 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Commons v. Pera


No. 45S00-1303-OR-209


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


987 N.E.2d 1074; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 373



May 17, 2013, Decided 
May 17, 2013, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator magistrates filed an action seeking both emergency and permanent writs of mandamus and prohibition regarding the filling of a vacancy on the bench in the Lake Superior Court, Juvenile Division, Indiana.


OVERVIEW: A majority voted to allow a judge to be reassigned from a county division to the juvenile division. This action seeking writ relief followed. In partially granting the requested relief, the supreme court determined that the prohibition against transfer in Ind. Code § 33-33-45-21(e) could have been enforced, despite a conflict with a transfer rule that was a part of an administrative order. The local transfer rule did not trump, by way of Ind. Code § 34-8-1-3, the prohibition on the transfer of non-eligible judges because the transfer rule was not a rule of the supreme court. Moreover, the supreme court disagreed with the argument that Ind. Code § 33-33-45-21(e) was unconstitutional under Ind. Const. art. 3, § 1. The Indiana Legislature had the power to create superior courts, and there was no authority for the argument that the Legislature was precluded from providing a method for the selection of the judges for the courts that it created. Finally, a permanent writ barring the transfer of any judge was not warranted because the transfer rule could have been used for efficiently operating and conducting the superior court's business.


OUTCOME: The request for a permanent writ was denied, but the other requested relief was granted.

 

39 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Doe


No. 49S00-1201-CT-14


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


987 N.E.2d 1066; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 370



May 14, 2013, Decided 
May 14, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D10-0402-CT-443. The Honorable David J. Dreyer, Judge. On Direct Appeal Pursuant to Indiana Appellate Rule 4(A)(1)(b).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a decision from the Marion Superior Court, Indiana, which determined that Ind. Code §§ 34-51-3-4, 34-51-3-5, and 34-51-3-6 were unconstitutional in a childhood sexual abuse case involving appellee injured party.


OVERVIEW: An injured party was awarded $150,000 in punitive damages as part of a judgment in a childhood sexual abuse case. The trial court denied a motion to reduce the punitive damages award after finding that there was a violation of Ind. Const. art. 3, § 1 and Ind. Const. art. 1, § 20. The State intervened in the case, and an appeal was filed. In reversing, the supreme court held that the jury's determination of the amount of punitive damages was not the sort of finding of fact that implicated the right to a jury trial under Ind. Const. art. 1, § 20. Moreover, the cap did not offend the separation of powers under Ind. Const. art. 3, § 1. Just as the legislative branch had broad power to limit common law causes of action and remedies, including punitive damages, the judicial branch had the sole authority to apply those limitations to particular cases. Finally, the cap and allocation provisions did not constitute a legislative remittitur. The cap did not require a choice between a reduced damages award and a new trial, nor did it depend upon the presence of some error in need of correction. The allocation provision did not change the amount of damage awarded.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions to grant a motion to reduce the punitive damages to the statutory maximum and to order that 75 percent of the award be deposited into the Violent Crime Victim Compensation Fund.

Girl Scouts of Southern Ill. v. Vincennes Ind. Girls, Inc.


No. 42S00-1210-PL-597


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


988 N.E.2d 250; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 371



May 14, 2013, Decided 
May 14, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Knox Circuit Court, No. 42C01-1005-PL-310. The Honorable Sherry B. Gregg Gilmore, Judge. On Direct Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grantee challenged a decision from the Knox Circuit Court, Indiana, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee grantor in a quiet title action.


OVERVIEW: The grantor deeded its campground to the grantee on the condition that Scouting use continue for 49 years. The deed provided that ownership of the campground reverted to the grantor of the Scouting use condition was breached during that time. After the use ceased, the grantor filed a suit seeking to quiet title in the campground. The grantee contended that the reversionary interest had expired by operation of Ind. Code § 32-17-10-2 or by terms of the deed when the grantor was administratively dissolved. The trial court granted summary judgment quieting title in favor of the grantor, and this appeal followed. In affirming, the supreme court determined that the grantor's corporate existence continued on a limited basis, even when it was administratively dissolved. Its reinstatement was retroactive as if the dissolution had never occurred. Next, Ind. Code § 32-17-10-2 was unconstitutional as applied under Ind. Const. art. 1, § 24. The contract between the parties would have been substantially impaired if the restriction were cut off after just 30 years. Arbitrary termination here was not within the necessary police power.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

41 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Meredith v. Pence


No. 49S00-1203-PL-172


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


984 N.E.2d 1213; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 229



March 26, 2013, Decided 
March 26, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D07-1107-PL-25402. The Honorable Michael D. Keele, Judge.
Meredith v. Daniels, 963 N.E.2d 1122, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 84 (Ind., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged a decision from the Marion Superior Court, Indiana, which granted summary judgment in favor of intervenor parents in an action challenging the constitutionality of the Indiana Choice Scholarship Program, Ind. Code §§ 20-51-4-1 to 20-51-4-11. A motion to transfer the case was granted.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers contended that the school voucher program was unconstitutional. Summary judgment was granted in favor of the parents, and a transfer of the case was granted. In affirming, the supreme court noted that the second duty under Ind. Const. art. 8, § 1, which was to provide for a general and uniform system of common schools, was not read to impose a restriction on the first duty, which was to encourage, by all suitable means, moral, intellectual, scientific, and agricultural improvement. The voucher program did not alter the structure or components of the public school system, and it fell under the duty to encourage. The General Assembly's specific task with performance standards fell under the duty to provide and was not implicated by the school voucher program. The taxpayers' argument improperly expanded the language of Ind. Const. art. I, § 4. Under Ind. Const. art. I, § 6, the school voucher program did not impermissibly provide direct benefits to religious institutions, and the phrase " religious or theological institutions" was not intended to apply to preclude government expenditures for functions, programs, and institutions providing primary and secondary education.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

42 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


M.L.B.K.J.R. v. M.A.B.


No. 41S01-1209-MI-00556


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


983 N.E.2d 583; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 170



March 7, 2013, Decided 
March 7, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Johnson Superior Court 1, No. 41D01-1007-MI-00031. The Honorable Kevin M. Barton, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 41A01-1107-MI-00285.
M.L.B. v. M.A.B., 969 N.E.2d 140, 2012 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 711 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court entered an order granting appellee grandfather visitation. Appellant mother appealed the visitation order. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed, and the state supreme court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: In awarding grandparent visitation, the trial court erred by failing to consider and enter findings required by Ind. Code § 31-17-5-6 all four Troxel factors. None of the trial court's findings gave any indication that it recognized the presumption that a fit parent acts in his or her child's best interests or that it gave special weight to a fit parent's decision to deny or limit visitation. Both factors were key to a constitutionally appropriate balance between a natural parent's fundamental rights and a child's best interests. The trial court erred by ordering extensive grandparent visitation, without the required findings to indicate why the mother's prior limitations on duration and the father's presence were unreasonable, or how the sudden increase in visitation would affect the child. Those omissions rendered the order unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the trial court for new findings and conclusions.

 

43 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


K.W. v. State


No. 49S02-1301-JV-20


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


984 N.E.2d 610; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 147



February 22, 2013, Decided 
February 22, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D09-1109-JD-2341. The Honorable Gary Chavers, Judge Pro Tempore, The Honorable Geoffrey Gaither, Magistrate. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1201-JV-9.
K.W. v. State, 976 N.E.2d 61, 2012 Ind. App. LEXIS 415 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, challenged a decision from the Marion Superior Court, Indiana, which adjudicated him delinquent for the offense of resisting law enforcement under Ind. Code § 35-44.1-3-1(a)(1) (2012). After the Indiana Court of Appeals reversed, the State sought a transfer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was about to get into a fight with another student. A school-liason officer arrived and attempted to handcuff defendant, who started to walk away and pulled on the officer's grasp. The school-liason officer was also a police officer. Defendant was adjudicated delinquent for resisting law enforcement. The Court of Appeals reversed, and the State sought a transfer. In reversing the trial court, the supreme court determined that there was insufficient evidence regarding whether defendant forcibly resisted, obstructed, or interfered with the officer. One forcibly resisted when strong, powerful means were used to evade a law enforcement official's rightful exercise of his or her duties. Merely walking away, leaning away from an officer's grasp, or twisting and turning against an officer's actions did not establish forcible resistance. Nothing in the description of defendant's actions suggested any strength, power, or violence in the maneuver, other than what was inherent in taking a step away.


OUTCOME: The trial court's delinquency adjudication was reversed and vacated.

 

44 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Plank v. Cmty. Hosps. of Ind., Inc.


No. 49S04-1203-CT-135


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


981 N.E.2d 49; 2013 Ind. LEXIS 60



January 15, 2013, Decided 
January 15, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Circuit Court, No. 49C01-0311-CT-3130. The Honorable Louis F. Rosenberg, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A04-1004-CT-254.
Plank v. Cmty. Hosps. of Ind., 956 N.E.2d 731, 2011 Ind. App. LEXIS 1846 (Ind. Ct. App., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Circuit Court, Indiana, denied plaintiff husband's motion seeking an evidentiary hearing to challenge the constitutionality of the Medical Malpractice Act (Act), Ind. Code §§ 34-18-1-1 to 34-18-18-2. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed the request for a hearing and affirmed the ruling on defendant hospital's jury instruction claim. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the husband's more immediate argument was that the trial court erred in denying him an evidentiary hearing so that he could develop a record to support his constitutional challenges. The record showed that by the time the husband's medical malpractice complaint proceeded to trial the matter had been pending for nearly six years. At no time prior to trial did the husband alert the trial court that he took exception to the cap imposed by the Act. The husband was well aware that the limitations of the cap applied to the case. He could have anticipated a motion to reduce the award in accordance with the cap in the event the jury returned an excess verdict. The husband forfeited any opportunity he otherwise may have been afforded to conduct an evidentiary hearing. He did not take the steps necessary to preserve his claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

45 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Bailey v. State


No. 49S02-1204-CR-234


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


979 N.E.2d 133; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 898



November 5, 2012, Decided 
November 5, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49G17-1101-FD-4920. The Honorable Clark Rogers, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1106-CR-487.
Bailey v. State, 961 N.E.2d 1047, 2012 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 117 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of domestic battery in violation of Ind. Code § 35-42-2-1.3(a)(1) (2008) and sentenced to two years in prison. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed. The court granted transfer to clarify the meaning of "bodily injury" and vacated the appellate court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The court held that no particular level of pain was required in order to rise to the level of impairment of physical condition and satisfy the definition of bodily injury under Ind. Code § 35-31.5-2-29, and that physical pain was an impairment of physical condition. The court further held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction because the victim, defendant's wife, testified that defendant shoved and repeatedly poked her in the forehead and that each of the acts caused her pain. The court held that defendant's two-year sentence was appropriate because he battered the victim while still on probation for a similar offense and he had a lengthy criminal history that included 11 prior adult convictions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

 

46 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Conley v. State


No. 58S00-1011-CR-634


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


972 N.E.2d 864; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 642



July 31, 2012, Decided 
July 31, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Conley v. State, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 869 (Ind., Oct. 22, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Ohio Circuit Court, Cause No. 58C01-0912-MR-001. The Honorable James Humphrey, Judge. On Direct Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Ohio Circuit Court (Indiana) sentencing him to life without parole after he pleaded guilty to murdering his 10-year-old brother.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was 17-and-a-half-year-old when he murdered his 10-year-old brother. The court held that the trial court did not err by allowing a State expert to testify that defendant had traits of a person with a diagnosis of psychopathy because the expert testified only on rebuttal to rebut the defense expert's testimony that defendant did not fit the psychotic personality. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in sentencing defendant to life in prison without parole because the aggravating factor of Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(b)(12) was clearly established and uncontrovered as defendant did not deny that his brother was only 10 years old when he died, and the trial court was within its discretion in weighing the mitigating factors, including that defendant was almost 18 years old and his lack of criminal history was offset by his smoking marijuana and drinking alcohol. The court held that the sentence was not inappropriate under Ind. R. App. P. 7(B) given the heinousness of the crime. The court held that the imposition of life without parole to a convicted murderer under the age of 18 in Indiana was in line with the rest of the nation in holding such a sentence was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was affirmed.

 

47 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Lock v. State


No. 35S04-1110-CR-622


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


971 N.E.2d 71; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 597



July 26, 2012, Decided 
July 26, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Huntington Superior Court, No. 35D01-0906-FD-135. The Honorable Jeffrey R. Heffelfinger, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 35A04-1010-CR-641.
Lock v. State, 952 N.E.2d 280, 2011 Ind. App. LEXIS 1360 (Ind. Ct. App., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed a petition to transfer from a split decision entered by the Indiana Court of Appeals, which reversed a superior court decision convicting defendant of operating a motor vehicle as a habitual traffic violator in violation of Ind. Code § 9-30-10-16(a). In granting a transfer, the court vacated the court of appeals' opinion.


OVERVIEW: Based on defendant's suspended driving privileges, he was only permitted to commute on a device with a maximum design speed of 25 miles per hour. While utilizing a motorized two-wheeled vehicle, defendant was pulled over after being clocked by radar at a speed of 43 miles per hour. At his trial, defendant stipulated to the police officer's radar reading. The court of appeals reversed defendant's conviction on the basis that the State's evidence of the vehicle's speed was too speculative. The court affirmed the conviction. Section 9-30-10-16(a) was not unconstitutionally vague because Ind. Code § 9-13-2-105(d) defined a motorized bicycle, which was not a motor vehicle under Chapter 9-30-10, as two-wheeled vehicle that did not have a maximum design speed of more than 25 miles per hour. While maximum design speed itself was not defined, a person of ordinary intelligence would understand that it referred to the speed based on the original manufacturer's design as well as any subsequent modifications or redesigns. Because defendant stipulated to the officer's radar reading, he could not argue that evidence of the vehicle's maximum design speed was speculative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

 

48 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Ward v. State


No. 74S00-0907-PD-320


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


969 N.E.2d 46; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 470



June 21, 2012, Decided 
June 21, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ward v. State, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 754 (Ind., Sept. 7, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Spencer Circuit Court, No. 74C01-0107-CF-158. The Honorable Robert J. Pigman, Special Judge. On Direct Appeal.
Ward v. State, 903 N.E.2d 946, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 343 (Ind., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner pleaded guilty in the Spencer Circuit Court (Indiana) to rape and murder and was sentenced to death. After his motion for post-conviction relief was denied, petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner argued, inter alia, that counsel performed deficiently in their mitigation investigation. The supreme court disagreed. Although counsel failed to discover some arguably mitigating evidence, they nevertheless conducted a reasonable investigation, thereby passing constitutional muster under U.S. Const. amend. VI. Counsel interviewed petitioner, his family members, and others who knew him to gain insight into his background and to develop his history; they also gathered records related to his education, his time in prison, and his mental health. Although counsel might have wanted to uncover certain mitigating evidence or might have intended to interview one potential mitigation witness in particular, they were not constitutionally deficient. Counsel sent one of her mental health experts all the police reports that had to do with the public indecency charges as well as all the probation records, education records, and prior mental health treatment evaluation records. Notwithstanding the absence of a completed social history, counsel prepared a three-page summary about petitioner based on his first trial, personal conversations with him, and his records.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

49 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Perdue v. Gargano


No. 49S02-1107-PL-437


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


964 N.E.2d 825; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 49



March 22, 2012, Decided 
March 22, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D10-0803-PL-13340. The Honorable David J. Dreyer, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-1003-PL-250.
Perdue v. Murphy, 938 N.E.2d 766, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 2249 (Ind. Ct. App., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, several classes and an individual applicant, challenged a decision from the Marion Superior Court, Indiana, which both granted and denied summary judgment in an action filed against appellee Secretary of the Family and Social Services Administration alleging violations of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, the Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990, 42 U.S.C.S. § 12101-12300, and 29 U.S.C.S. § 794.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied summary judgment as to 2 classes after finding that the Indiana Family and Social Services Administration's (FSSA) process for eligibility determination satisfied due process. It granted summary judgment to a third class after finding that federal food stamp law only permitted a denial of benefits where there was a refusal to cooperate, and it enjoined the FSSA from denying, terminating, or discontinuing benefits based on an alleged failure to cooperate. It granted summary judgment on a reasonable accommodation claim concerning the individual applicant. This appeal followed. In partially affirming, the supreme court held that the notices used by the FSSA to inform individuals that their applications for Medicaid, Food Stamp, or Temporary Assistance to Needy Families benefits had been denied did not satisfy the requirements of due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Next, federal law permitted the FSSA to deny benefits to applicants who failed to cooperate in the eligibility determination process. The individual applicant was entitled to reasonable accommodation, but the provision of a caseworker or case management services was not required.


OUTCOME: The decision was partially affirmed as to reasonable accommodation to the individual applicant, but summary judgment was reversed as to third class on the food stamp claim. Summary judgment was granted on the due process issue. The case was remanded for further proceedings.


 

50 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



State v. IBM


No. 49S00-1201-PL-15


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


964 N.E.2d 206; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 42



March 21, 2012, Decided 
March 21, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by State v. IBM, 2014 Ind. App. LEXIS 54, 4 N.E.3d 696 (Ind. Ct. App., 2014)
Related proceeding at State v. Marion Superior Court, 2016 Ind. LEXIS 473 (Ind., July 5, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D10-1005-PL-021451. The Honorable David J. Dreyer, Judge. Civil Transfer of Appeal of Interlocutory Order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State of Indiana, sought interlocutory review of an order from the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) compelling the Governor's deposition in a contract dispute brought by the State against appellee contractor.


OVERVIEW: The contractor undertook to provide services to a state agency. The Governor, along with several other officials, signed the contract and made public statements concerning it. Thereafter, the State gave notice that it was terminating the contract. The parties filed claims against one another. The contractor served notice on the Governor to take his testimonial deposition pursuant to Ind. R. Trial P. 30(A). The trial court issued a protective order but later granted a motion to compel the Governor's deposition. The court held that Ind. Code Ann. § 34-29-2-1(6) provided an absolute privilege that, upon being invoked, precluded a deposition of a sitting governor. Although the statute referred to a "subpoena," the court noted that the policy behind the privilege encompassed protection from all manner of interference with official duties and that notice of a deposition provided all that was necessary for a subpoena to issue under Ind. R. Trial P. 45(D). Thus, a notice of a deposition accomplished essentially the same goals as a subpoena and was privileged in essentially the same manner. Legislative intent to provide a gubernatorial privilege against compelled testimony was clear.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

White v. Ind. Democratic Party


No. 49S00-1202-MI-73


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


963 N.E.2d 481; 2012 Ind. LEXIS 29



March 15, 2012, Decided 
March 15, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 16, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Circuit Court, No. 49C01-1012-MI-55881. The Honorable Louis Rosenberg, Judge.
White v. Ind. Democratic Party, 963 N.E.2d 1118, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 197 (Ind., Feb. 7, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Circuit Court, Indiana, reversed the decision of appellant Indiana Recount Commission (Commission) finding that dismissed appellee's, political party's chairman, claim seeking to have appellant candidate in a state election declared ineligible to assume office. The Commission and candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court had to determine whether the political party's petition was a basis for barring the candidate's taking office after winning the election. The supreme court had to determine whether the claim was still valid under Ind. Code § 3-12-11-2 when brought following the general election, or if it had to have been brought earlier under Ind. Code § 3-8-8-1. When the challenge was filed post-election the candidate was correctly registered at his place of residence in the election district he sought to represent, i.e., the State of Indiana. The allegations of the candidate's registration impropriety arose before the election and were made public. The political party did not point to anything in their briefs to demonstrate the impossibility of their discovering the candidate's registration issues at an earlier date.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the Commission's dismissal was affirmed.


 

52 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



State v. Econ. Freedom Fund


No. 07S00-1008-MI-411


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


959 N.E.2d 794; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 1096



December 29, 2011, Decided 
December 29, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Econ. Freedom Fund, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 33 (Ind., Mar. 14, 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Freeeats, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 6942 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Brown Circuit Court, No. 07C01-0609-MI-0425. The Honorable Kenneth G. Todd, Special Judge. On Petition to Transfer Pursuant to Appellate Rule 56(A).
FreeEats.com, Inc. v. Indiana, 502 F.3d 590, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 21823 (7th Cir. Ind., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a decision from the Brown Circuit Court, Indiana, which granted a preliminary injunction in favor of appellee, a company making political calls, in dispute over the constitutionality of the Indiana Autodialer Law, Ind. Code §§ 24-5-14-1 to -13 (2007).


OVERVIEW: The State contended that the company had violated Ind. Code § 24-5-14-5(b). The company argued that the law violated Ind. Const. art. 1, § 9 and U.S. Const. amend. I. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the company, and this appeal followed. In reversing, the supreme court determined that an alternative federal free speech argument was not before it. A review of a grant or denial of a preliminary injunction was confined to the law applied by the trial court. The supreme court only evaluated the merits of arguments reached by the trial court. The holding in Mitchell v. Mitchell was not extended to orders granting or denying preliminary injunctions. At any rate, the claim under U.S. Const. amend. I would have likely failed. Next, the trial court improperly found that the company had a reasonable likelihood of success on its claim that the live-operator requirement violated Ind. Const. art. 1, § 9. The live-operator requirement did not impose a substantial obstacle on the company's right to engage in political speech. A purely economic burden was not the type of substantial obstacle that was contemplated by Price v. State.


OUTCOME: The trial court's denial in part of the preliminary injunction in favor of the State was reversed. The trial court's granting in part of a preliminary injunction in favor of the company was also reversed.


 

53 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Snyder v. King


No. 94S00-1101-CQ-50


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


958 N.E.2d 764; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 1091



December 15, 2011, Decided 
December 15, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certified Question from the United States District Court for the Southern District of Indiana. No. 1:10-cv-1019-WTL-MJD. The Honorable William T. Lawrence, Judge.
Snyder v. King, 941 N.E.2d 1043, 2011 Ind. LEXIS 577 (Ind., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an declaratory judgment action filed by a former inmate, the United States District Court for the Southern District of Indiana certified a question to the supreme court, as revised: (1) whether misdemeanor battery is an "infamous crime" under Ind. Const. art. II, § 8; and (2) if not, whether cancellation of former inmate's voter registration violated the Indiana Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The Indiana General Assembly had authority under its general police power to disenfranchise persons incarcerated upon conviction of a crime, so long as the disenfranchisement lasts only for the duration of incarceration. That Ind. Code §§ 3-7-13-4, 3-7-13-5(a), 3-7-46-2 cited the Infamous Crimes Clause, Ind. Const. art II, § 8 as the basis for its enactment, instead of the general police power, did not render it invalid. The Indiana Constitution was not violated when, upon being convicted of Class A misdemeanor battery and sentenced to an executed term of incarceration, former inmate was disenfranchised but only for the duration of his incarceration. The General Assembly had separate constitutional authority to cancel the registration of any person incarcerated following conviction, for the duration of incarceration. The General Assembly could exercise its police power to deprive all convicted prisoners of the right to vote for the duration of their incarceration. The loss of political and civil rights upon conviction and imprisonment was simply a collateral consequence thereof.


OUTCOME: The General Assembly had constitutional authority to cancel the voter registration of any person incarcerated following conviction, for the duration of incarceration.


 

54 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Garcia-Torres v. State


No. 64S03-0912-CR-550


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


949 N.E.2d 1229; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 575



June 30, 2011, Decided 
June 30, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Porter Superior Court, No. 64D01-0505-FB-4871. The Honorable Roger V. Bradford, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 64A03-0812-CR-630.
Garcia-Torres v. State, 914 N.E.2d 268, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 2043 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Porter Superior Court (Indiana) that convicted him of rape, attempted rape, and two counts of burglary, arguing that the use of DNA evidence violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV and Ind. Const. art. 1, § 11, because the DNA was procured through a cheek swab taken without his consent or probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was questioned by police because he matched the description of a man who had sexually assaulted several women. After the interview, a detective asked if he could swab the inside of defendant's cheek for DNA, and defendant consented. Defendant's DNA matched the DNA from a victim's rape kit and DNA from a victim's apartment. On review, the court held that the taking of the DNA swab of defendant's cheek did not violate defendant's rights against unreasonable searches and seizures because defendant's consented to the DNA swab and his consent was voluntarily given. While defendant argued that his consent was not voluntarily given because the police did not advise him of his Miranda rights, the Fourth Amendment did not require Miranda warning before officers ask for consent to search. The court rejected defendant's additional argument that his consent could not be deemed voluntary because he was only 18 years of age; defendant's mental state or condition (other than a mental state caused by police coercion) was not a factor in determining voluntariness. Further, defendant failed to show any coercive police conduct sufficient to vitiate the voluntary nature of his consent.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

 

55 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


A.B. v. State


No. 71S00-1002-JV-156


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


949 N.E.2d 1204; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 567



June 29, 2011, Decided 
June 29, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ind. Dep't of Child Servs. v. A.B., 2011 Ind. LEXIS 994 (Ind., Nov. 1, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the St. Joseph County Probate Court, No. 71J01-0811-JD-000826. The Honorable Peter Nemeth, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, challenged a decision from the St. Joseph County Probate Court, Indiana, which entered an order of modification of a dispositional order and declared three statutes to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: At a placement hearing, it was recommended that the juvenile be placed at a facility in Arizona. The cost of this placement was less than the cost at facilities in Indiana. The trial court determined that Ind. Code §§ 31-37-17-1.4, 31-37-18-9(a) and (b), and 31-40-1-2(f), as amended by Pub. L. No. 182-2009ss, § 387 (Ind.), were all unconstitutional, and this appeal followed. On review, the supreme court determined that the statutes at issue were constitutional under Ind. Const. art. 3, § 1. Moreover, Ind. Code § 31-40-1-2(f), as amended by Pub. L. No. 182-2009ss, § 387 (Ind.), was constitutional under Ind. Const. art. 4, § 19. The disapproval of payment for an out-of-state placement was subject to appellate review, but only upon an arbitrary and capricious showing. The focus was what was in the best interest of the juvenile in light of all of the circumstances of a particular case. Because the disapproval decision was arbitrary and capricious, then the Indiana Department of Child Services (DCS) was responsible for payment as if it has approved the recommendation.


OUTCOME: The child was ordered to continue the placement in Arizona, and the DCS was ordered to pay for that placement.

 

56 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Lemmon v. Harris


No. 52S02-1011-CV-642


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


949 N.E.2d 803; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 566



June 28, 2011, Decided 
June 28, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Miami Circuit Court, No. 52C01-0709-MI-644. The Honorable Robert A. Spahr, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 52A02-0911-CV-1088.
Williams v. State Emples. Appeals Comm'n, 2011 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 395 (Ind. Ct. App., Mar. 29, 2011)
Buss v. Harris, 926 N.E.2d 1110, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 787 (Ind. Ct. App., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Commissioner of the Indiana Department of Correction (DOC), challenged a judgment of the Indiana Court of Appeals that affirmed a circuit court's declaratory judgment and injunctive relief for plaintiff sex offender and removed his status as a sexually violent predator (SVP).


OVERVIEW: The sex offender was convicted of a sex offense before the Indiana Legislature created the SVP status. After the status was created, the DOC branded the sex offender an SVP. The sex offender filed suit, seeking a declaratory judgment finding that the DOC lacked the authority to make an SVP determination. On review of the lower court judgments in favor of the sex offender, the court held that Ind. Code § 35-38-1-7.5 applied retroactively to the sex offender. Specifically, because the sex offender was convicted of child molesting as a Class B felony (one of the qualifying offenses) and because he was released from incarceration after June 30, 1994, the statute provided that his status was that of an SVP by operation of law. Further, that change in status did not violate Indiana's prohibition on ex post facto laws or doctrine of separation of powers. The SVP provision of the 2007 Amendment advanced the Indiana Sex Offender Registration Act's legitimate regulatory purpose of public safety, and only those people who presented a future threat were required to register for their lifetimes.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the appellate court and circuit court were reversed. The sex offender's status as an SVP came into effect by operation of law, and that classification did not violate the Indiana Constitution.
