Barnes v. State


No. 82S05-1007-CR-343


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


946 N.E.2d 572; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 353



May 12, 2011, Decided 
May 12, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing granted by Barnes v. State, 2011 Ind. LEXIS 851 (Ind., Sept. 20, 2011)
Clarified by, On rehearing at Barnes v. State, 2011 Ind. LEXIS 815 (Ind., Sept. 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Vanderburgh Superior Court, No. 82D02-0808-CM-759. The Honorable Mary Margaret Lloyd, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 82A05-0910-CR-592.
Barnes v. State, 925 N.E.2d 420, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 627 (Ind. Ct. App., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Vanderburgh Superior Court, Indiana, which convicted him of misdemeanor battery on a law enforcement officer, misdemeanor resisting arrest, and misdemeanor disorderly conduct. The Indiana Court of Appeals ordered a new trial on the battery and resisting charges, and the case was transferred.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's wife called 911 when an altercation arose as he was moving out of their apartment. Officers approached defendant outside the apartment, and he began yelling. When defendant returned to the apartment, he refused to let the officers inside. As one officer attempted to enter and investigate, defendant shoved him against the wall. Defendant was convicted of various charges, but the court of appeals ordered a new trial. A transfer then occurred. In affirming, the supreme court determined that the trial court did not err by failing to tender a jury instruction on the right of a citizen to reasonably resist unlawful entry into a citizen's home. The right to resist an unlawful police entry into a home was against public policy and was incompatible with modern U.S. Const. amend. IV jurisprudence. The right to reasonably resist an unlawful police entry into a home was no longer recognized under Indiana law. Finally, the evidence was sufficient to support the convictions for disorderly conduct, battery on a law enforcement officer, and resisting arrest.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

58 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



State ex rel. Zoeller v. Aisin USA Mfg.


No. 36S01-1009-CV-469


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


946 N.E.2d 1148; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 354



May 12, 2011, Decided 
May 12, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State ex rel. Zoeller v. Aisin USA Mfg., 2011 Ind. LEXIS 789 (Ind., Sept. 13, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Jackson Superior Court, No. 36D01-0905-MI-8. The Honorable Bruce Markel, III, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 36A01-0909-CV-442.
State ex rel. Zoeller v. Aisin USA Mfg., 926 N.E.2d 83, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 717 (Ind. Ct. App., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Indiana Attorney General challenged a decision from the Jackson Superior Court, Indiana, which granted appellee taxpayer's motion to dismiss an action seeking to collect money due to an erroneous refund for lack of jurisdiction. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed, and a transfer was granted.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer contended that the superior court lacked subject matter jurisdiction over the Attorney General's attempt to recover an erroneously issued tax refund. A motion to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction was granted. The court of appeals affirmed, and the case was then transferred. In reversing, the supreme court determined that the superior court had subject matter jurisdiction over all civil and criminal cases, except where exclusive jurisdiction had been conferred by law upon a different court with the same territorial jurisdiction, pursuant to Ind. Code § 33-33-36-4 (2008). The tax court had exclusive subject matter jurisdiction over original tax appeals, pursuant to Ind. Code § 33-26-3-3 and Ind. Tax Ct. R. 13. Here, the case could have proceeded in the superior court because it did not arise under the tax laws under Ind. Code § 33-26-3-1. The refund was the result of accounting and clerical errors within the Indiana Department of Revenue that were wholly unrelated to any interpretation or application of tax law. There was no limitation by the tax laws on the ability to seek restitution from the taxpayer in this case.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded for proceedings on the merits of the claims.


 

59 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



City of Indianapolis v. Armour


No. 49S02-1007-CV-402


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


946 N.E.2d 553; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 350



May 10, 2011, Decided 
May 10, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Armour v. City of Indianapolis, 132 S. Ct. 576, 181 L. Ed. 2d 420, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 8180 (U.S., 2011)
Affirmed by Armour v. City of Indianapolis, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 4131 (U.S., June 4, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D11-0706-CT-026050. The Honorable John F. Hanley, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0901-CV-84.
City of Indianapolis v. Armour, 918 N.E.2d 401, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 2669 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought a transfer after the Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed a decision from the Marion Superior Court, Indiana, relating to the forgiveness of an assessment.


OVERVIEW: The city decided to forgive outstanding assessments due to a transition from Barrett Law, Ind. Code § 36-9-39-1 to 36-9-39-30 (2007), to the Septic Tank Elimination Program (STEP). No refunds were granted to those who had paid the assessment already, and this action was filed. The trial court entered judgment for the city, and the court of appeals affirmed. This transfer followed. In reversing, the supreme court determined that Indianapolis, Ind., Board of Public Works Resolution 101 was rationally related to its legitimate interests in reducing its administrative costs, providing relief for property owners experiencing financial hardship, establishing a clear transition from Barrett Law to STEP, and preserving its limited resources. Moreover, this was not a class of one case since the Resolution made a broad classification on the basis of a common characteristic. Finally, there was no evidence that the city's action was motivated by animus or ill-will toward property owners who did not have outstanding assessments.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision was reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions to grant judgment in favor of the city on the federal equal protection claim.


 

60 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Love v. Rehfus


No. 30S01-1004-CV-162


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


946 N.E.2d 1; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 331; 32 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 316



April 21, 2011, Decided 
April 21, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Love v. Rehfus, 2011 Ind. LEXIS 669 (Ind., July 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Hancock Circuit Court, No. 30C01-0705-PL-493. The Honorable Richard D. Culver, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 30A01-0905-CV-250.
Love v. Rehfus, 918 N.E.2d 448, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 2678 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a wrongful termination action, plaintiff firefighter appealed summary judgment granted by the Hancock Circuit Court (Indiana) in favor of defendants, a township and its fire department, arguing that his employment was improperly terminated where the conduct that led to his termination was protected speech under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: During a primary election campaign, the firefighter received an email from an acquaintance regarding one candidate's position on issues that would affect the fire department. The firefighter sent a response to a citizens group supporting one politician's candidacy and discussed several issues. Eventually, a copy of the email was received by the fire department chief, who thereafter terminated the firefigher's employment. The firefighter filed suit, but the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the township and fire department by concluding that the employee's speech was not protected under the First Amendment. On review, the court held that the trial court erred in granting summary judgment because the firefighter was engaged in protected speech under the Pickering test. The email supported a political candidate for public office and was sent in response to an email that was critical of the firefighter's preferred candidate. The email was sent in the midst of a debate on public issues and was sent from the firefighter's home computer, and defendants failed to show any harm or disruption to the operation of the fire department caused by the email.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order granting summary judgment in favor of defendants was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

61 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Killbuck Concerned Citizens Ass'n v. J.M. Corp.


No. 48S00-1003-PL-158


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


941 N.E.2d 1037; 2011 Ind. LEXIS 70



February 10, 2011, Decided 
February 10, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Madison Superior Court, No. 48D01-0806-PL-996. The Honorable Michael D. Keele, Special Judge.
Office of Envtl. Adjudication v. J.M. Corp., 691 N.E.2d 449, 1997 Ind. App. LEXIS 1790 (Ind. Ct. App., 1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Madison Superior Court, Indiana, granted appellee corporation's motion for summary judgment and denied appellant residents' association's motion, holding that while the association and appellant landowner had standing and Ind. Code § 13-20-2-11 applied to the corporation, the statute violated the Indiana Constitution because it was a special law that could have been made to apply generally. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the dispositive issue under Ind. Code § 13-20-2-11 (Supp. 2008) was whether the corporation's facility accepted waste before the applicable deadline. The supreme court noted that the language of the statute was clear; it applied only to a facility that did not accept waste before the applicable deadline. The statute did not apply to the corporation. The corporation's facility included both its landfill (disposal operation) and collection container system (storage operation). The corporation's facility accepted waste by receiving it willingly in exchange for money. The household trash accepted at the corporation's facility was waste for purposes of Ind. Code § 13-11-2-205 (2004). Because the corporation's facility accepted waste before April 1, 2008, Ind. Code § 13-20-2-11 did not apply.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


 

62 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Caesars Riverboat Casino, LLC v. Kephart


No. 31S01-0909-CV-403


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


934 N.E.2d 1120; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 560



September 30, 2010, Decided 
September 30, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Harrison Circuit Court, No. 31C01-0701-CC-005. The Honorable H. Lloyd Whitis, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 31A01-0711-CV-530.
Caesars Riverboat Casino, LLC v. Kephart, 903 N.E.2d 117, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 514 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant casino sued appellee patron for unpaid counter checks. The patron countersued. The Harrison Circuit Court, Indiana, denied the casino's motion to dismiss, but certified its ruling for interlocutory appeal. The Indiana Court of Appeals accepted jurisdiction and reversed the trial court's judgment. Transfer was granted.


OVERVIEW: The counter checks were returned to the casino for insufficient funds, and the casino filed suit. The patron counterclaimed, alleging that the casino knew of the patron's pathological addiction and took advantage of the addiction for gain. The casino moved to dismiss the counterclaim under Ind. R. Trial P. 12(B)(6). The reviewing court held that the trial court erred in denying the casino's motion to dismiss the patron's counterclaim under Ind. R. Trial P. 12(B)(6) for failure to state a claim upon which relief could be granted. It appeared that by unmistakable implication the Indiana Legislature had abrogated any common law claim that casino patrons might otherwise have against casinos for damages resulting from enticing patrons to gamble and lose money at casino establishments. The Legislature did not require casinos to identify and refuse service to pathological gamblers who did not self-identify. The existence of the voluntary exclusion program suggested the Legislature intended pathological gamblers to take personal responsibility to prevent and protect themselves against compulsive gambling.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

63 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Leone v. Comm'r, Ind. BMV


No. 49S02-0910-CV-505


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


933 N.E.2d 1244; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 520



September 10, 2010, Decided 
September 10, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-0802PL-6305. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0804-CV-377.
Leone v. Comm'r, 906 N.E.2d 172, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 821 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, who were a class of driver's licensees, sought review of a decision of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana), which denied their motion for a preliminary injunction against appellee, the Indiana Bureau of Motor Vehicles (Bureau). The licensees had set forth the action by asserting that the Bureau had overstepped its authority by defining "legal name" to exclude anything but the name on file with the Social Security Administration.


OVERVIEW: The Bureau notified almost 200,000 people that their driver's licenses and identification cards did not match their Social Security records. Within six months, more than three-quarters of those people had somehow corrected the discrepancies. The licensees at issue represented the subset of those remaining whose names did not match, which was about 15,000 people. The court upheld the denial of the preliminary injunction because the licensees had not established a likelihood of success on the merits. Specifically, Ind. Code § 9-24-9-2(a)-(b) required the Bureau to maintain the information on the applications and authorized it to suspend or revoke the current driving license of any person for any reasonable ground appearing on the records of the Bureau. As the Social Security Administration had become the custodian of Americans' basic identifying information and almost all state governments relied on that information to verify identities, the Bureau had logically decided to verify the identities of those with licenses and identification cards with the Social Security Administration. The Bureau was within its authority to depend on Social Security to maintain the verifiable names.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.


 

64 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



League of Women Voters of Ind., Inc. v. Rokita


No. 49S02-1001-CV-50


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


929 N.E.2d 758; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 412



June 30, 2010, Decided 
June 30, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D13-0806-PL-27627. The Honorable S.K. Reid, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0901-CV-40.
League of Women Voters of Ind., Inc. v. Rokita, 915 N.E.2d 151, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 1628 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the League of Women Voters, appealed an order of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) that dismissed their claim against defendant, the Indiana Secretary of State, in their declaratory action seeking to have the Indiana Voter ID Law declared invalid for violating Ind. Const. art. 2, § 2, and Ind. Const. art. 1, § 23.


OVERVIEW: The League of Women Voters filed suit seeking a declaration that it was beyond the power of the legislature to require voters to identify themselves at the polls using a photo ID. On review of the order of dismissal, the court held that voters have long been required to identify themselves at the polls by announcing and signing their names, and neither of the constitutional provisions invoked by the League of Women Voters prevented the legislature from promulgating a new way for voters to identify themselves. It was within the power of the legislature to require voters to identify themselves at the polls using a photo ID. As such, the claim for relief could not be granted, and dismissal was appropriate. No individual voter had alleged that the Voter ID Law prevented him or her from voting or inhibited his or her ability to vote in any way, and the court's decision would not prevent any voter adversely affected by the Voter ID Law from challenging the law in the face of such adverse effect.


OUTCOME: The order of dismissal was affirmed.

Whatley v. State


No. 49S02-0908-CR-379


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


928 N.E.2d 202; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 382



June 8, 2010, Decided 
June 8, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by Whatley v. State, 937 N.E.2d 1238, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 2220 (Ind. Ct. App., 2010)
Post-conviction relief denied at Whatley v. State, 969 N.E.2d 634, 2012 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 738 (Ind. Ct. App., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion County Superior Court, No. 49G20-0803-FD-64067. The Honorable Michael Jensen, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0809-CR-808.
Whatley v. State, 906 N.E.2d 259, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 837 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Marion County Superior Court, Indiana, which convicted him of possession of cocaine as a Class A felony under Ind. Code § 35-48-4-6. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed and remanded the case with instructions to enter the conviction as a Class C felony.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of cocaine as a Class A felony since the offense occurred within 1,000 feet of a youth program center. The jury found that the enhancement was supported by the evidence, and defendant was sentenced to a term of 35 years in prison. The court of appeals reversed on the grounds that the building at issue did not qualify as a youth program center. In vacating the decision from the court of appeals, the supreme court rejected defendant's argument that Ind. Code § 35-41-1-29 was vague. The statute did not have to be free of ambiguities, but had to put a person of ordinary intelligence on notice or provide objective criteria for determining whether one was within a protected area. It did not matter that the building did not have a sign indicating that it was a youth program center or that defendant did not realize that the building regularly provided services and programs to young people. The conviction for the Class A felony was supported by sufficient evidence because the building regularly offered recreational, social, and other programs for persons less than 18 years of age. Finally, the 35-year sentence was appropriate under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The opinion from the court of appeals was vacated. Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Richardson


No. 49S02-0910-CR-428


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


927 N.E.2d 379; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 380



June 3, 2010, Decided 
June 3, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion County Superior Court, No. 49G20-0707-FA-135662. The Honorable William Young, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0807-CR-583.
State v. Richardson, 906 N.E.2d 263, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 891 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee driver was stopped for not wearing a seat belt, a violation of the Indiana Seatbelt Enforcement Act, Ind. Code § 9-19-10-3.1. He was subsequently arrested for having a firearm with a prior felony conviction, and was charged with firearm and cocaine related crimes and resisting arrest. The Marion County Superior Court, Indiana, granted the driver's motion to suppress; the Indiana Court of Appeals reversed and the driver sought transfer.


OVERVIEW: The officer who stopped the driver knew him from a previous traffic stop and testified that he was cooperative and admitted he was not wearing a seat belt. In calling in the driver's information, the officer was told that he had a prior felony. During his arrest, the driver tried to escape. The court held that a vehicle, the contents of a vehicle, the driver of a vehicle, or a passenger in a vehicle could not have been inspected, searched, or detained solely because of a violation of Ind. Code § 9-19-10-3.1, the Indiana Seatbelt Enforcement Act. A traffic stop based solely upon the failure of the driver or the passenger to wear a seat belt did not provide reasonable suspicion for the officer to unilaterally expand an investigation. Although an officer could conduct a limited search or inquiry concerning weapons without obtaining a search warrant if the officer reasonably believed that he or others might have been in danger that was not the circumstance herein. The court did remand for further findings concerning whether the driver's conduct created separate probable cause to arrest him for forcibly resisting arrest and battery upon a law enforcement officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's findings concerning the suppression of the cocaine found on the driver as a result of the search conducted pursuant to the seat belt violation; the court remanded the case to the trial court for further findings concerning the driver's conduct while he resisted arrest.

Carter v. Nugent Sand Co.


No. 49S00-0812-CV-00649


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


925 N.E.2d 356; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 261



April 14, 2010, Decided 
April 14, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-0710-PL-42564. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Indiana Department of Natural Resources (DNR), appealed the judgment of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) that entered a permanent injunction in favor of appellees, land owners and lessees, declaring that a lake and channel were private property and barring the DNR from informing that the lake and channel were open to the public. The court denied DNR's motion to dismiss. DNR appealed.


OVERVIEW: DNR argued that instead of filing the present court action, the lessee should have undertaken remedies for an interpretation of the dedication to public use requirement of Ind. Code § 14-29-4-5(2) and the application of that statue to the property at issue. The supreme court agreed. The lessee had an administrative remedy. Indeed, each permit contained information about how to appeal. Moreover, the terms imposed by DNR were explicitly set forth in the "special conditions" section of the approval documents. As for whether that language was adequate to alert the lessee to the fact that it was giving up exclusive use by virtue of obtaining the permits, there might have been some basis for debating whether the statute and the permit conditions applied to the channel and the lake or just to the channel. But the lessee forcefully insisted that it gave up nothing at all ("even a single boater getting 'in the way' is an unacceptable interference") when the statute was plain that at least the channel ("all additional waters") was being dedicated to public use. DNR gave plain enough notice.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with directions to grant the motion to dismiss.

Treadway v. State


No. 49S00-0803-CR-147


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


924 N.E.2d 621; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 243



April 8, 2010, Decided 
April 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, Criminal Division 5, No. 49G05-0511-MR-188188. The Honorable Grant W. Hawkins, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Marion Superior Court, Criminal Division 5 (Indiana) of murder, felony murder, robbery, and battery. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his convictions. The court of appeals disagreed. On the night her husband was murdered, the witness spoke with two different officers. One testified that she was the first officer on the scene and spoke with the witness who told her "the subject was a white male with gray hair, a salt and pepper beard and a plaid jacket." The witness's stepson testified that the witness told him that her attacker "was defendant who had done yard work for them in the past." At trial the witness testified that the person who attacked her and her husband was defendant. Also, the witness identified the artist's sketch of defendant that had been previously introduced as an exhibit, and pointed defendant out for the jury. In sum, the State presented an eyewitness who identified defendant in court, made a sketch of defendant shortly after the murder, and described defendant to at least three people shortly after the attack. Contrary to defendant's claim, the evidence was sufficient to prove that defendant was the person that attacked the victim and the witness.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Schlechty


No. 38S04-0905-CR-246


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


926 N.E.2d 1; 2010 Ind. LEXIS 208



March 24, 2010, Decided 
March 24, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 1, 2010.
Rehearing denied by State v. Schlechty, 2010 Ind. LEXIS 415 (Ind., June 30, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Schlechty v. Indiana, 131 S. Ct. 934, 178 L. Ed. 2d 776, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 302 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Jay Superior Court, No. 38D01-0806-CM-095. The Honorable Joel D. Roberts, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 38A04-0810-CR-572.
State v. Schlechty, 2009 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 276 (Ind. Ct. App., Feb. 12, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: While defendant was on probation following a burglary conviction, a warrantless search of his vehicle revealed marijuana and drug paraphernalia. Defendant was charged with possession of marijuana as a Class A misdemeanor. A trial court (Indiana) granted his pre-trial motion to suppress the evidence. The State appealed. A divided panel of the Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed. Transfer was granted.


OVERVIEW: As a condition of probation, defendant agreed to submit to reasonable warrantless searches of his person and/or property by his probation officer in conjunction with other law enforcement officers. He was also required to behave well and not commit any criminal offense. After receiving reports that defendant was trying to lure a young girl into his car, defendant's probation officer and other officers searched his car without a warrant. The probation officer was not sure if defendant had committed a criminal offense, but he believed that defendant was not behaving well. The court held that a warrantless search of a probationer's property that was conducted reasonably, supported by a probation search term and reasonable suspicion of criminal activity, complied with the dictates of the Fourth Amendment. Here, defendant's conduct implicated the criminal offenses of stalking and attempted confinement. Viewed objectively, the officers had reasonable suspicion to believe criminal activity had occurred. The warrantless search was supported both by reasonable suspicion to believe that defendant engaged in criminal activity and a search condition contained in his probation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the cause.

State v. Hernandez


No. 45S00-0806-CR-377


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


910 N.E.2d 213; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 792



July 30, 2009, Decided 
July 30, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Lake Superior Court, No. 49G02-7505-CR-111. The Honorable Clarence D. Murray, Judge. The Honorable T. Edward Page, Temporary Judge.
Gutierrez v. State, 270 Ind. 639, 388 N.E.2d 520, 1979 Ind. LEXIS 615 (1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Indiana appealed from judgments of the Lake Superior Court (Indiana), which found that Ind. Code § 11-13-3-2(b)(3) (Supp. 1979) was unconstitutional as applied to petitioner inmate. The matter arose in the inmate's post-conviction relief (PCR) petition. The inmate also appealed from the trial court's determination that there was no sentencing issue. The inmate had been convicted and sentenced for two murders.


OVERVIEW: The inmate and others robbed and killed two victims. He was convicted and sentenced, which decision was affirmed on appeal. The inmate filed a successive PCR petition, asserting that he was only sentenced to one life term, and that the Parole Board had improperly applied Ind. Code § 11-13-3-2(b)(3) (Supp. 1979) to his 1975 convictions, such that he was denied consideration for parole. He claimed that such application violated the ex post facto clauses of both the United States and Indiana Constitutions. The trial court held that § 11-13-3-2(b)(3) was unconstitutional as applied to the inmate. On appeal, the court found that the sentencing issue was waived where it was not raised on direct appeal, and the fundamental error exception did not apply. There were no ex post facto implications by application of § 11-13-3-2(b)(3) to the inmate, as he was not otherwise eligible for parole. Based on Ind. Code §§ 11-1-1-9 and 11-1-1-9.1 (1971, 1974), an individual serving a life sentence in 1975 was not eligible for parole because such sentence was neither determinate nor indeterminate. The court overruled Johnston v. Dobeski, 739 N.E.2d 121 (Ind. 2000) to the extent that it conflicted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial in part of the inmate's PCR petition, and it reversed that part of the trial court decision that granted the PCR petition. The court remanded the matter to the trial court with instructions to enter judgment for the State.

Jackson v. State


No. 48S02-0809-CR-513


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


908 N.E.2d 1140; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 507



June 30, 2009, Decided 
June 30, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Madison Superior Court, Division 1, No. 48D01-0608-FB-270. The Honorable Dennis D. Carroll, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 48A02-0711-CR-988.
Jackson v. State, 889 N.E.2d 830, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1408 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon. The Madison Superior Court (Indiana) sentenced defendant to 13 years executed with credit for time served pending trial. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court erred in denying his pretrial motion to suppress. The supreme court disagreed, finding that the search warrant for defendant's home was valid under the good faith exception to the warrant requirement. Assuming without deciding that probable cause did not exit to support issuing a warrant to search defendant's home, the supreme court nonetheless concluded that the trial court correctly overruled defendant's motion to exclude evidence. Nothing in the record suggested that the detective misled the trial court when testifying at the hearing to obtain the warrant; thus, the issue was whether the detective's testimony was so bare bones and lacking in indicia of probable cause as to make a reliance upon the warrant objectively unreasonable. The supreme court concluded that the detective's testimony was sufficient, as it demonstrated the nature of his relationship with the informant through evidence of the informant's involvement in other controlled buys. The detective also bolstered the informant's tip by testifying to complaints from the public about traffic consistent with drug dealing.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

72 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Mosley v. State


No. 49S02-0812-CR-643


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


908 N.E.2d 599; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 500



June 26, 2009, Decided 
June 26, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49F10-0711-CM-242464. The Honorable Linda E. Brown, Judge. The Honorable Louis F. Rosenberg, Magistrate. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0802-CR-188.
Mosley v. State, 893 N.E.2d 1186, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2045 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of resisting law enforcement; he appealed, challenging the sufficiency of the evidence. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed; it suggested that counsel faced with preparing an appeal in which they had identified no issue of merit should file an "Anders brief." The high court granted transfer to address the latter issue.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that discussion of the Anders issue would constitute only an advisory opinion. The high court noted that Ind. Const. art. VII, § 1 did not preclude it from issuing advisory opinions. As the issue had significant implications for appellate practice, constitutional rights, legal ethics, and management of judicial resources, and was likely to recur, the high court proceeded to the merits. It found that Anders was cumbersome and inefficient. Requiring counsel to submit an ordinary appellate brief the first time--no matter how frivolous counsel regarded the claims--was quicker, simpler, and placed fewer demands on the appellate courts. Relying on its supervisory authority over matters of appellate procedure and professional responsibility, the high court held that in any criminal appeal as a matter of right, counsel could neither withdraw on grounds that the appeal was frivolous nor submit an Anders brief; counsel had to submit an advocative brief in accordance with Ind. R. App. P. 46. The high court disapproved Packer v. State, 777 N.E.2d 733 (Ind. Ct. App. 2002), and Seals v. State, 846 N.E.2d 1070 (Ind. Ct. App. 2006), to the extent they held otherwise.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. The opinion of the intermediate appellate court was summarily affirmed as to the sufficiency of the evidence supporting defendant's conviction.

 

73 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Bonner v. Daniels


No. 49S02-0809-CV-525


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


907 N.E.2d 516; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 472



June 2, 2009, Decided 
June 2, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D01-0604-PL-16414. The Honorable Cale J. Bradford, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0702-CV-188.
Bonner v. Daniels, 885 N.E.2d 673, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 959 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students, by their relatives and next friends, appealed a judgment by the Court of Appeals (Indiana) that reversed the dismissal of their complaint for a declaratory judgment to establish that the Indiana Constitution imposed an enforceable duty on defendant state government to provide a standard of quality education to public school students and that such duty was not being satisfied.


OVERVIEW: The students sought a judicial declaration that Indiana's system of school financing violated three provisions of the Indiana Constitution: the Education Clause (Article 8, § 1), the Due Course of Law Clause (Article 1, § 12), and the Equal Privileges and Immunities Clause (Article 1, § 23). Critical to their claims was their contention that the Indiana Constitution created an enforceable duty on state government to provide an adequate quality education. The state supreme court found, inter alia, that while there was strong evidence of the students' general concerns, they pointed to no historical evidence that the framers intended more than their historic accomplishment of requiring the legislature to establish a system of free common schools in the Education Clause. In addition, neither the Equal Privileges nor Due Course of Law Clauses of the Indiana Constitution created a constitutional right to be educated to a certain quality or other output standard. Accordingly, the students were not entitled to the declaratory relief sought.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


 

74 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Jensen v. State


No. 02S04-0803-CR-137


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


905 N.E.2d 384; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 400



April 30, 2009, Decided 
April 30, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D04-9903-CF-129. The Honorable Frances C. Gull, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 02A04-0706-CR-351.
Jensen v. State, 878 N.E.2d 400, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 2935 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Indiana) affirmed a finding that defendant, who had been found guilty of vicarious sexual gratification and was released from prison in 2001, had to report and register as a sexually violent predator for life under Ind. Code § 11-8-8-19 (2006), instead of for the 10-year period previously required. On appeal, he claimed that § 11-8-8-19's lifetime registration requirement, as applied to him, was an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: The court found that defendant did not carry his burden of proving that the effect of the Indiana Sex Offender Registration Act was punitive and violated the ex post facto prohibitions of U.S. Const. art. 1, § 10 and Ind. Const. art. 1, § 24. Of the seven Mendoza-Martinez factors that were relevant to the inquiry of whether a statute had a punitive effect despite legislative intent that the statute be regulatory and non-punitive, only three leaned in favor of treating the effects of the Act as punitive. The seventh factor (excessiveness in relation to the State's articulated purpose), to which the court gave the most weight, favored treating the Act's effects as non-punitive. The "broad and sweeping" disclosure requirements were in place and applied to defendant when he pled guilty in 2000. Ind. Code § 11-8-8-19's lifetime registration requirement did not impose a greater burden or have a greater adverse affect on him. Ind. Code § 35-38-1-7.5(g) allowed defendant to petition a court to determine whether he had to remain classified as a sexually violent predator. His direct appeal of his sentence was foreclosed because the trial court exercised no discretion in sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Post-conviction relief denied at Ward v. State, 969 N.E.2d 46, 2012 Ind. LEXIS 470 (Ind., 2012)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Dismissed by, Judgment entered by Ward v. Wilson, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 126406 (S.D. Ind., Sept. 22, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Spencer Circuit Court, No. 74C01-0107-CF-158. The Honorable Robert J. Pigman, Judge.
Ward v. State, 810 N.E.2d 1042, 2004 Ind. LEXIS 610 (Ind., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the sentence of death imposed against him by the Spencer Circuit Court (Indiana), following his conviction for the 2001 rape and murder of a 15-year-old girl. Defendant had pled guilty to the rape and murder charges. A penalty phase jury determined that a death sentence should be imposed, and the trial court sentenced defendant accordingly, as required by Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(e)(2).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of Indiana's death penalty statute; the jury selection process; the admission of evidence from a warrantless search; the admission of photographic evidence; and the appropriateness of the death sentence. The admission of evidence the police collected from his person following his arrest but before a search warrant was issued was upheld as an authorized search incident to a lawful arrest as well as proper under the exigent circumstances exception. The court agreed that the victim's autopsy photos were gruesome but that each was accompanied by the testimony of a forensic pathologist explaining the nature of the medical procedures performed. The court found the death sentence appropriate in light of the horrendous crime committed and the proof of torture and mutilation that was established.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment ordering the death sentence for defendant.

Pruitt v. State

SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


903 N.E.2d 899; 2009 Ind. LEXIS 338

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Pruitt v. State, 907 N.E.2d 973, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 491 (Ind., 2009)
Rehearing denied by Pruitt v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 797 (Ind., June 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1]

Pruitt v. State, 834 N.E.2d 90, 2005 Ind. LEXIS 822 (Ind., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the denial of his petition for post-conviction relief in the Dearborn Circuit Court (Indiana). He was sentenced to death for the murder of a police officer. His conviction and sentence were upheld on direct appeal.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court considered appellant's numerous claims that his conviction and death sentence had to be vacated because his lawyers' performance at trial was so deficient that it denied him the effective assistance of counsel guaranteed by the Sixth Amendment to the federal Constitution. Trial counsel did not render ineffective assistance before a pre-trial mental retardation hearing with respect to developing and presenting evidence of appellant's adaptive functioning, as counsel presented the testimony of appellant's neighbor when appellant was growing up, and appellant's aunt, on the areas of work and self-direction. Counsel obtained qualified mental health experts who competently advanced a diagnosis of mental retardation, as a defense expert found that appellant had substantial deficits in the six adaptive behaviors of communication. A court-appointed expert on mental retardation corroborated this conclusion. Evidence that appellant's IQ score was elevated by an anti-psychotic was not previously undiscovered evidence pursuant to Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(k) because it was cumulative of a defense expert's testimony on this same issue during appellant's capital murder trial.


OUTCOME: The post-conviction court's denial of appellant's petition for post-conviction relief was affirmed.

State v. Washington


No. 02S03-0804-CR-191


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


898 N.E.2d 1200; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 1284



December 31, 2008, Decided 
December 31, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Washington, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 624 (Ind., May 14, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D04-0607-CM-5058. The Honorable Robert E. Ross, Magistrate. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 02A03-0703-CR-124.
State v. Washington, 875 N.E.2d 278, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 2370 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence in a criminal case charging him with possession of marijuana. The State dismissed the charges and appealed pursuant to Ind. Code § 35-38-4-2(5). The Court of Appeals (Indiana) affirmed the trial court in a published opinion. The court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether, under both U.S. Const. amend. IV and Ind. Const. art. 1, § 11, an officer, without reasonable suspicion, could ask about possible drug possession when defendant was stopped for a traffic infraction. From the court's review of U.S. Supreme Court jurisprudence, the court concluded that the officer's conduct did not violate the Fourth Amendment. The officer saw defendant driving a moped across the center line and conducted a traffic stop also to investigate defendant's possible failure to wear safety equipment required by Ind. Code § 9-21-11-13. The brief questioning as to whether defendant had any weapons, drugs, or anything else that could harm the officer was not a search or seizure and was not prohibited by the Fourth Amendment. Defendant did not have to answer the questions; his choice to do so and to disclose inculpatory information provided the basis for the officer's further request for permission to search defendant's pants pockets. The contraband question, notwithstanding the absence of reasonable suspicion, was not unreasonable under the totality of the circumstances and did not violate Ind. Const. art. 1, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court granting the motion to suppress and remanded the cause.

Harris v. State


No. 48S02-0812-CR-637


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


897 N.E.2d 927; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 1277



December 11, 2008, Decided 
December 11, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Madison Circuit Court, No. 48C01-0306-FA-192. The Honorable Fredrick R. Spencer, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 48A02-0606-CR-532.
Harris v. State, 881 N.E.2d 733, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 422 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of child molesting. The trial court ordered two consecutive sentences of 50 years. The Court of Appeals of Indiana affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant committed his crimes before the sentencing statute was amended, and thus the presumptive sentencing scheme applied. The trial court identified three aggravating circumstances, and no mitigating factors. However, the trial court did not explain why the aggravating circumstances warranted consecutive sentences. Thus, the appellate court held that the trial court fell short of the requirement that it explain its reasons for selecting the sentence imposed. Defendant was in a position of trust with the child victim, who believed defendant was her biological father. The trial court also found that defendant molested the victim on multiple occasions other than the crimes charged. The ongoing nature of defendant's crimes coupled with his position of trust was sufficiently aggravating to justify enhanced sentences. Although defendant's criminal history was not inconsequential, his convictions were not significant aggravators. The aggravating circumstances were sufficient to warrant imposing enhanced sentences for child molesting, but were not sufficient to justify imposing consecutive sentences.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court was summarily affirmed with respect to defendant's convictions. Defendant's sentence was revised to 50 years for each count of child molesting and the sentences were ordered to have been served concurrently.

Herron v. Anigbo


No. 45S03-0811-CV-594


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


897 N.E.2d 444; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 1051



November 13, 2008, Decided 
November 13, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Herron v. Anigbo, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 119 (Ind., Feb. 10, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Lake Circuit Court, No. 45C01-0503-CT-00040. The Honorable Lorenzo Arredondo, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 45A03-0608-CV-378.
Herron v. Anigbo, 866 N.E.2d 842, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 1080 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Lake Circuit Court (Indiana) granted summary judgment in favor of appellee doctor because the two-year occurrrence-based statute of limitations, Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b) (2004), barred appellant patient's Indiana Medical Malpractice Act claim. The circuit court found that the patient knew or should have known of the malpractice from another doctor's report and that the remaining period was a reasonable time to file a claim.


OVERVIEW: Although the court had not specifically addressed the procedure to be followed in a malpractice case when a limitations issue could not have been resolved on summary judgment, the court agreed with the prevailing view in other jurisdictions that factual issues surrounding the statute of limitations in medical malpractice cases were for a trier of fact. The court found that summary judgment was appropriate. Claims based on negligence in a May 6, 2002 surgery were barred because the patient failed as a matter of law to pursue his claim with reasonable diligence within the period required by the statute. It was understandable that the patient's focus was on medical outcomes, not legal remedies, in the more than two years following the surgery; but, the legislature prescribed an occurrence-based limitations period, and the patient was charged with knowledge of the law, however unrealistic that assumption may be in a given case. Because the patient's evidence provided the date of his discharge from a hospital as May 31, 2002, more than two years before the complaint was filed, the claim for negligent follow-up or monitoring was also barred by the occurrence-based limitation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment.

Norris v. State


No. 43S03-0807-CR-379


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


896 N.E.2d 1149; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 1048



November 12, 2008, Decided 
November 12, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Kosciusko Circuit Court, No. 43C01-0408-FC-134. The Honorable Rex L. Reed, Judge. On Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 43A03-0708-CR-396.
Norris v. State, 881 N.E.2d 691, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 366 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Four years after his guilty plea, conviction, and sentence for child molesting, defendant inmate sought post-conviction relief on grounds of newly discovered evidence. The State's motion for summary disposition was granted, and the inmate appealed. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed and remanded for an evidentiary hearing before the post-conviction court. The state's highest court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that the inmate sought to vacate his conviction based on his sister's recantation of facts that were presented to police and led to his arrest and criminal charges. However, the inmate's conviction was based only on his guilty plea. It was not based on his sister's testimony. The new position taken by the inmate could not be harmonized with the fact that he originally admitted to committing the crime by his guilty plea. When the trial court accepted the inmate's guilty plea, he waived the right to present evidence as to guilt or innocence. This was a waiver under Ind. R.P. Post-Conviction Remedies 1, §§ 1(a)(1), 8. The inmate's claim that because of his limited verbal and non-verbal skills, low intellectual functioning, and mild mental retardation, his sister could convince him to say what she wanted him to say did not challenge the knowing and voluntary nature of his plea. He did not seek to withdraw his plea. The inmate had already served his sentence; he sought only to set aside and vacate his conviction. A guilty plea could not be challenged in post-conviction proceedings by a claim of newly discovered evidence claim on the events of the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Membres v. State


No. 49S02-0701-CR-33


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


889 N.E.2d 265; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 492



June 27, 2008, Decided 
June 27, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Membres v. State, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 811 (Ind., Sept. 4, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49G20-0510-MC-185560. The Honorable Michael Jensen, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0511-CR-1065.
Membres v. State, 851 N.E.2d 990, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 1465 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a circuit court granted the State's Ind. Code § 35-33-5-5(j) motion to transfer property seized from defendant's trash pursuant to a search warrant, the Court of Appeals (Indiana) found that the search was unlawful under the standard announced in Litchfield. That court did not reach defendant's claim that the warrant was overbroad. The court accepted transfer of the case.


OVERVIEW: Litchfield was decided two weeks after the search and expressly adopted the requirement of articulable individualized suspicion as an elaboration of Moran, which held that warrantless trash searches were valid under Ind. Const. art. 1, § 11, under the totality of the circumstances, if officers conducted themselves in the same manner as trash collectors. Litchfield reshaped the understanding of what constituted a reasonable warrantless trash search. No court had determined whether principles of retroactivity applied to the exclusion of evidence produced from an unconstitutional search or seizure. The court agreed with the Skidmore court that where a new rule was designed to deter illegal police conduct, the new rule was not to be applied retroactively. The Litchfield rule was designed to deter random intrusions into the privacy of all citizens. Retroactive application would not advance its purpose because deterrence operated prospectively. The court found that the seizure of trash did not violate the Fourth Amendment because defendant had no expectation of privacy in the trash outside his house and under the totality of the circumstances, the search was reasonable under Moran.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to suppress and affirmed the decision granting the State's motion for a turnover order.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Lake County Superior Court, No. 45G01-0507-FA-0035. The Honorable Salvador Vasquez, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 45A03-0605-CR-211.
Campos v. State, 867 N.E.2d 676, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 1210 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Indiana appellate court, in a case where defendant was charged with dealing in cocaine, affirmed the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress cocaine found during a search of a vehicle in which he was a passenger. However, it affirmed the appellate court's affirmance of the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress the statements made in the police cruiser. Also, it ordered that the case be remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Smith v. Ind. Dep't of Corr.


No. 49S02-0804-CV-166


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


883 N.E.2d 802; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 316



April 9, 2008, Decided 
April 9, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Smith v. Knight, 2009 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1020 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D01-0511-CT-43581. The Honorable Cale J. Bradford, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0601-CV-58.
Smith v. Ind. Dep't of Corr., 853 N.E.2d 127, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 1672 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Indiana Court of Appeals entered a judgment that upheld the Three Strikes Law, Ind. Code § 34-58-2-1, against plaintiff inmate's challenge that it violated the Open Courts Clause, Ind. Const. art. I, § 12, and, thus, affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the inmate's civil complaint. The state supreme court granted transfer of the case to it.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed a civil complaint to challenge his treatment at a correctional facility after he had to be subdued. The trial court dismissed the complaint pursuant to the Three Strikes Law, Ind. Code § 34-58-2-1 (Three Strikes Law), because three prior lawsuits the inmate filed were dismissed as frivolous under the Frivolous Claim Law, Ind. Code § 34-58-1-2. Although the inmate challenged the Three Strikes Law as a violation of the Open Courts Clause, Ind. Const. art. I, § 12 (Open Courts Clause), the appellate court affirmed. The state supreme court found that the Three Strikes Law violated the Open Courts Clause. It determined that the Indiana Constitution did not balance the inconvenience of entertaining a claim against the right to seek redress subject to reasonable conditions. It noted that the right to petition the courts was absolute, which meant that an individualized assessment of each claim was required, and a claim could not be dismissed because of who presented it rather than whether it had merit. Otherwise, the state supreme court reasoned, a meritorious claim could be barred simply because an individual previously had filed too many frivolous claims.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing the complaint under the Three Strikes Law. It then ordered that the case be remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Baxendale v. Raich

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's judgment that modified physical custody to provide that appellee father would have such child custody while appellant mother lived in another state. The state supreme court granted transfer.

It found modification warranted to keep the minor son in his present location because relocation would be difficult for an 11-year-old already adjusted to his current community. 86 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Brinkman v. Bueter


No. 29S02-0704-CV-141


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


879 N.E.2d 549; 2008 Ind. LEXIS 42



January 15, 2008, Decided 
January 15, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Hamilton Superior Court, No. 29D01-0410-CT-872. The Honorable Steven David, Special Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 29A02-0510-CV-980.
Brinkman v. Bueter, 856 N.E.2d 1231, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 2377 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a husband and a wife, brought a medical malpractice suit against defendants, physicians and a professional corporation. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendants except on the issue of negligent counseling. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed the grant of summary judgment and affirmed the denial of summary judgment as to negligent counseling. The present court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: In 1995, the wife was treated for eclampsia. That same year, she was told that a future pregnancy would be high risk. In 2000, the wife, after becoming pregnant, consulted another physician, who told them that the wife had not received proper care in 1995 and that the advice she had been given about future pregnancies was incorrect. The court held that plaintiffs' claims were time-barred under Ind. Code Ann. § 34-18-7-1(b) (2007), which was an "occurrence" statute as opposed to a "discovery" statute. Although it had been held that the statute violated the Indiana Constitution's Privileges and Immunities and Open Courts clauses when applied to patients who suffered from diseases or medical conditions with long latency periods, this was not the case here. The wife was diagnosed and treated for eclampsia in 1995, and nothing prevented plaintiffs from bringing a claim for faulty diagnosis and treatment within the two-year statutory period. As for the negligent counseling claim, the alleged negligence occurred in 1995. Nothing prevented plaintiffs from seeking further medical or legal advice. Thus, this claim was also time-barred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendants. It directed entry of judgment for defendants on the counseling claim.


 

87 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Prewitt v. State


No. 10S04-0707-CR-294


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


878 N.E.2d 184; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 1120



December 18, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Clark Superior Court, No. 10D02-0001-CF-017. The Honorable Cecile A. Blau, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 10A04-0610-CR-589.
Prewitt v. State, 865 N.E.2d 669, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 864 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In response to defendant's probation violation, a trial court had executed a portion of his previously suspended sentence and modified the conditions of probation. The Court of Appeals of Indiana reversed the judgment, directing that defendant be resentenced. A petition to transfer from the lower court was granted.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the provisions of Ind. Code Ann. § 35-38-2-3(g) (2007) governing probation violations permitted a trial judge to order execution of one portion of a previously suspended sentence and continuation of another portion as probation under modified terms. Because the subsections in § 35-38-2-3(g) were connected by the word "or," defendant argued that the trial court could order only one of the three alternatives. Although the disjunctive "or" normally expressed a legislative intent that only one of the enumerated options was permitted, giving the term its ordinary meaning flew in the face of a clearly contrary legislative intent. Specifically, recent legislative activity evinced an intent to encourage judicial flexibility in response to probation violations. Thus, § 35-38-2-3(g) permitted judges to sentence offenders using any one of or any combination of the enumerated options. Defendant's sentence for the probation violation was not an abuse of discretion where he had repeatedly violated his probation and repeatedly failed to complete a halfway house program.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision was affirmed.

 

88 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Ritchie v. State


No. 49S00-0409-PD-420


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


875 N.E.2d 706; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 996



November 8, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ritchie v. State, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 148 (Ind., Feb. 20, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Direct Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, Criminal Division, Room 4. No. 49G04-0010-CF-172900. The Honorable Patricia Gifford, Judge.
Ritchie v. State, 809 N.E.2d 258, 2004 Ind. LEXIS 476 (Ind., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Marion Superior Court, Criminal Division (Indiana), which denied his petition for postconviction relief following the affirmance of his convictions for murder, possession of a handgun, auto theft, and resisting law enforcement in connection with the shooting death of a police officer. He also challenged his death sentence for the murder conviction.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether appellant was denied the effective assistance of trial or appellate counsel. Affirming the judgment, the court held that appellant did not receive ineffective assistance at trial as (1) a decision to rely upon testimony from the State's witness was reasonable and a failure to procure another expert after problems arose with the original defense expert did not overcome a presumption of competence; (2) because the admission of videotaped statements did not violate appellant's Miranda rights as the statements were made to the media, a failure to object to the introduction of such statements did not constitute ineffective assistance; and (3) during the guilt phase, trial counsel sufficiently investigated appellant's medical, educational, family, and social history. Appellate counsel was not ineffective as (1) counsel was entitled to make a judgment call concerning which issues to raise on appeal; and (2) even assuming that a failure to raise an Ind. R. App. P. 7(B) challenge to the death sentence fell below an objective standard of reasonableness, appellant did not show a reasonable probability that such issue would have resulted in the revision of his sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's judgment.

 

89 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Azania


No. 02S03-0508-PD-364


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


875 N.E.2d 701; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 997



November 7, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Interlocutory Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D04-8109-CF-401. The Honorable Steve David, Special Judge.
State v. Azania, 865 N.E.2d 994, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 328 (Ind., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of Indiana filed a petition for rehearing after the supreme court reversed a ruling of the Allen Superior Court (Indiana) that prohibited the State from seeking a death penalty sentence for appellee inmate for a third time. The State sought a clarification of which iteration of Indiana's death penalty statute applied to the new sentencing phase.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted in 1982 of the 1981 murder of a police officer. His original death sentence was in 1982. He was resentenced to death in 1996. Amendments to the Indiana death penalty statute, Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9, were made in 1993 and 2002. Prior supreme court decisions had held that the 2002 amendment, which required judges to sentence defendants in accordance with the sentencing recommendation of a jury, was procedural, not substantive in nature. Considering those decisions, the supreme court clarified its prior order by indicating that the inmate's new sentencing proceeding was to be conducted pursuant to the post-2002, death penalty statute, Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9 (Supp. 2006). However, in accordance with Pub. L. No. 250-1993, § 3 (Ind.), and prior case law, a sentence of life without parole (LWOP) was not available to the inmate as a sentence, even though it was available under the post-2002 version of the death penalty statute. Pub. L. No. 250-1993, § 3 (Ind.) specifically stated that LWOP was to be available only in sentencing phases for defendants who committed their crimes after June 30, 1993.


OUTCOME: The supreme court granted the State's petition for rehearing and clarified its prior ruling. The supreme court denied a petition for rehearing filed by the inmate.

State v. McManus


No. 82S00-0503-PD-78


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


868 N.E.2d 778; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 496



June 27, 2007, Decided 
June 27, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. McManus, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 903 (Ind., Sept. 28, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McManus v. Indiana, 552 U.S. 1298, 128 S. Ct. 1739, 170 L. Ed. 2d 543, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2983 (2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied McManus v. Wilson, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 34893 (S.D. Ind., Mar. 31, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Vanderburgh Circuit Court, No. 82C01-0102-CF-192. The Honorable William J. Brune, Special Judge.
McManus v. State, 814 N.E.2d 253, 2004 Ind. LEXIS 780 (Ind., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant was found guilty of murdering his wife and children and sentenced to death, he filed a petition for postconviction relief. The Vanderburgh Circuit Court (Indiana) largely rejected his claims, except for his contention that he was mentally retarded. Persuaded by his retardation claim, the postconviction court vacated the sentence of death and entered a sentence of life without parole. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's mental retardation claim was preserved even though he failed to follow the existing statutory procedure as the claim had not ripened until after the court had modified Indiana's statutory scheme to comply with the Atkins rule. Defendant did not meet the Ind. Code § 35-36-9-2 standard where two IQ tests and the expert testimony showed that he was not below the level of intellectual functioning defining mental retardation. Defendant also had not proved substantial impairment of adaptive behavior where he successfully held down three jobs, his test scores did not reflect impairment in the conceptual, social, and practical domains, and he had no difficulty caring for his invalid daughter. The lower court's admission of expert testimony was not a ground for reversal as the testimony was not particularly harmful to the State. Defendant's ineffective assistance of counsel claims failed as his trial counsel had sought a competency hearing and had conducted a substantial investigation of mitigating evidence. Finally, defendant had not shown that trial counsel had an conflict of interest as there was no evidence that counsel's loyalty belonged to anyone other than defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed as to the holding on mental retardation, but was otherwise confirmed. The appellate court entered judgment for the State.

Anglemyer v. State


No. 43S05-0606-CR-230


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


868 N.E.2d 482; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 490



June 26, 2007, Decided 
June 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Corrected by Anglemyer v. State, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 539 (Ind., July 10, 2007)
Rehearing granted by Anglemyer v. State, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 1006 (Ind., Oct. 30, 2007)
Modified by, On rehearing at Anglemyer v. State, 875 N.E.2d 218, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 962 (Ind., Oct. 30, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Kosciusko Superior Court, No. 43D01-0505-FB-76. The Honorable Duane G. Huffer, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 43A05-0510-CR-590.
Anglemyer v. State, 845 N.E.2d 1087, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 662 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, pursuant to a written plea agreement, pleaded guilty in the Kosciusko Superior Court (Indiana) to robbery as a Class B felony and battery as a Class C felony. The trial court imposed a ten-year sentence for the robbery conviction and a six-year sentence for the battery conviction, with the sentences to run consecutively. Defendant appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and then beat and kicked a pizza delivery driver in a robbery. The plea agreement into which defendant entered provided that the sentence would not exceed 16 years executed. Defendant contended that the maximum possible sentence imposed under the plea agreement was inappropriate in light of his character and the nature of the offenses. The state supreme court observed that Indiana's new sentencing statutes created a regime in which there was no longer a maximum sentence a judge could impose without any additional findings because the maximum sentence under Ind. Code § 35-38-1-7.1 (Supp. 2005) was then the upper statutory limit. As a result, even with judicial findings of aggravating circumstances, it was impossible to increase the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum. Further, the trial court's sentencing statement under Ind. Code § 35-38-1-3 (Supp. 2005) sufficiently identified for appellate review aggravating and mitigating factors and explained why they were deemed as such. Finally, defendant did not show an abuse of discretion as to the sentence in a review under Ind. R. App. P. 7(B). Thus, the sentence imposed was not improper.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Israel v. Ind. Dep't of Corr.


No. 46S03-0706-CV-253


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


868 N.E.2d 1123; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 493



June 26, 2007, Decided 
June 26, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the LaPorte Superior Court, No. 46D03-0410-SC-1351. The Honorable Paul J. Baldoni, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 46A03-0509-CV-421.
Israel v. Ind. Dep't of Corr., 854 N.E.2d 1220, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 2005 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate appealed a decision of the LaPorte Superior Court (Indiana), which denied his claim to recover funds withdrawn by defendant Indiana Department of Correction (DOC) from his inmate trust account for restitution purposes. The DOC sought dismissal of the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was given disciplinary segregation and ordered to pay $ 8,363 in restitution after he stabbed a corrections officer. A class action settlement check for $ 2,800 was deposited in the inmate's trust account. The DOC withdrew the money from his trust account for restitution purposes. The inmate filed a small claims action alleging that the withdrawal violated the terms of his written agreement with a prison counselor that purported to limit the amount the DOC could withdraw from his account. The trial court decided the case against him. The inmate appealed. The court granted the inmate's petition for transfer and held that the trial court should have dismissed the case for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The court held that the restitution was a prison disciplinary sanction, was an agency action related to an offender within the jurisdiction of the DOC, pursuant to Ind. Code § 4-21.5-2-5(6), and, thus, was not subject to judicial review. The court held that any arrangement the DOC may have made with the inmate to specify how the sanction would be met was also an agency action not subject to judicial review.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's petition for transfer and determined that the trial court should have dismissed the action.

Giles v. Brown County


No. 03S01-0605-CV-175


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


868 N.E.2d 478; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 486



June 25, 2007, Decided 
June 25, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Bartholomew Circuit Court, No. 03C01-0210-CT-1306. The Honorable Stephen R. Heimann, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 03A01-0502-CV-00087.
Giles v. Brown County, 839 N.E.2d 1258, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 2446 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the surviving spouse of a decedent and representative of the decedent's estate (estate), which sued defendant county for negligently failing to provide requested medical services, appealed the grant of summary judgment by the Bartholomew Circuit Court (Indiana) in favor of the county on the grounds of immunity. The Court of Appeals reversed and remanded the matter. The county sought, and was granted, transfer.


OVERVIEW: The decedent called 911 to request an ambulance when he experienced chest pains and shortness of breath at his home. The enhanced emergency communication system (E-911) forwarded his request to a hospital's ambulance service. No ambulance reserved for the county's use was available, so the hospital instead contacted another hospital and asked it to dispatch an ambulance. Decedent eventually died. The Indiana Supreme Court noted that the estate did not allege that a constitutionally protected right is at stake, nor did it allege that the spouse was a member of a suspect class. Therefore, the rational basis test applied. Because, in both the case of a conventional E-911 service and a wireless one, statutory immunity from liability was a reasonable and rational way for the Legislature to have furthered its legitimate interest in better emergency communication systems, the estate's claim that Ind. Code § 34-13-3-3(19) violated equal protection principles was rejected. The estate fared no better under Ind. Const. art. I, § 23. She failed to meet her burden to demonstrate the statutes' unconstitutionality.


OUTCOME: The trial court's grant of summary judgment to the county was granted.


 

94 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. State


No. 49S05-0612-CR-494


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


868 N.E.2d 464; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 477



June 22, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49F09-0407-FD-137884 The Honorable Mark Stoner, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A05-0506-CR-321.
Brown v. State, 848 N.E.2d 699, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 1067 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions by the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) for three counts of criminal confinement and three counts of identity deception. The Court of Appeals reversed the criminal confinement convictions based on Ind. Code § 35-42-3-3(a) (2004) being unconstitutionally vague. It affirmed the convictions for identity deception and remanded for sentencing. Both defendant and the State sought transfer, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant telephoned at least three adult men and falsely informed them of a radio contest in which they could each win a prize if they would drive from their job to defendant's residence, remove all of their clothes and exchange them for a T-shirt. Each of the three counts of criminal confinement charged that defendant knowingly used "fraud" or "enticement" to remove the victims from one place to another. The Indiana Supreme Court held that Ind. Code § 35-42-3-3(a) would occur whenever a person knowingly or intentionally arouses hope or desire in another person to lure or attract the other to change location. Because § 35-42-3-3(a) failed to indicate where the line was to be drawn between trivial and substantial things so that erratic arrests and convictions for trivial acts and omissions would not occur, the statute was unconstitutionally vague. While there was evidence that defendant used information identifying the corporate radio station without its consent, there was no evidence that he used the name, address, date of birth, or other identifiers of any existing human being in perpetrating his hoax. Thus, he could not be convicted under Ind. Code § 35-43-5-3.5(a) (2004).


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for criminal confinement and identity deception were each reversed.
