Lambert v. State


Supreme Court Case No. 18S00-0412-SD-503


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


867 N.E.2d 134; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 358



May 21, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Application denied by Lambert v. Ind., 127 S. Ct. 2970, 168 L. Ed. 2d 289, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 7723 (U.S., June 14, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Delaware Superior Court Case No. 18D01-9101-CF-02.
Lambert v. State, 675 N.E.2d 1060, 1996 Ind. LEXIS 189 (Ind., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Delaware Superior Court (Indiana) convicted petitioner of murder. Petitioner was sentenced to death. After the conviction and sentence were affirmed on direct appeal and subsequent collateral attacks, petitioner brought a proceeding against respondent State, seeking authorization to file a second successive post-conviction petition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner shot a police officer to death after the officer arrested petitioner. The appellate court found that petitioner had already availed himself of the rule that permitted a person convicted of a crime in an Indiana state court one collateral review of the conviction and sentence in a post-conviction proceeding. Because he had completed the review to which he was entitled as a matter of right, he needed the appellate court's authorization to litigate another or "successive" post-conviction claim. Petitioner claimed that the death sentence was unconstitutional because, in the course of the litigation, a majority of the current sitting members of the appellate court had dissented as to the propriety of the death sentence. However, in each of the appellate court's decisions addressing the death sentence, a majority of the members of the appellate court voted to deny him the sentencing relief he requested. At every stage of the litigation before the appellate court, petitioner had received the individualized treatment to which he was entitled. A case cited by petitioner was not available to him and even if it were, he would not have been entitled to relief.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.

 

96 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Azania


No. 02S03-0508-PD-364


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


865 N.E.2d 994; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 328



May 10, 2007, Decided  
May 10, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Superseded by, Rehearing denied by State v. Azania, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 997 (Ind., Nov. 7, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Interlocutory Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D04-8109-CF-401. The Honorable Steve David, Special Judge. On Interlocutory Appeal.
Azania v. State, 778 N.E.2d 1253, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 890 (Ind., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a murder conviction and death sentence, appellee inmate filed a petition for post-conviction relief. The case was remanded for a new penalty phase. A second death sentence was imposed. The inmate filed a second petition for post-conviction relief and the case was again remanded for a new penalty phase. The Allen Superior Court (Indiana) granted the inmate's motion to prohibit the State from seeking the death penalty. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate argued that allowing the State to seek a death sentence in a new penalty phase trial would violate his speedy trial and due process rights because evidence that supported his case was no longer available and the jury would likely focus on future dangerousness. The supreme court held that the inmate had the burden of demonstrating that some action on the part of the State delayed his appeal. The supreme court further found that, when balanced against the inmate's own actions, the State's improper failure to comply with discovery orders did not delay the new penalty phase trial for purposes of a speedy trial analysis; the inmate's own requests to disqualify local judges and to conduct additional discovery delayed the new penalty phase trial for an even longer period of time independent of the State's improprieties. As for the inmate's due process claim, the supreme court held that the prejudice he would suffer because of the unavailability of mitigating witnesses identified by the inmate was not substantial enough to violate his due process rights; among other things, every one of the witnesses identified by the inmate as being unavailable were the State's witnesses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded for a new penalty phase trial.

 

97 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Naugle v. Beech Grove City Sch.


No. 49S02-0606-CV-242


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


864 N.E.2d 1058; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 283



April 27, 2007, Decided  
April 27, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Circuit Court, No. 49C01-0402-PL-490. The Honorable Theodore M. Sosin, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0505-CV-472.
Naugle v. Beech Grove City Schs., 840 N.E.2d 854, 2006 Ind. App. LEXIS 40 (Ind. Ct. App., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employees brought an action against defendant school corporation in which plaintiffs alleged that defendant had violated the Wage Payment Statute, Ind. Code § 22-2-5-1 to 22-2-5-3. The trial court granted partial summary judgment to defendant. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed. Plaintiffs appealed, and the court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: The court first held that the Wage Payment Statute applied to school corporations. Under Ind. Code § 22-2-5-1(a), the Statute applied to persons and corporations, and Ind. Code § 1-1-4-5(17) extended the definition of "person" to include "bodies politic and corporate." Nothing in the statute supported exempting school employees from its protection. Next, the court rejected defendant's argument that there was a good faith exception to the Statute. The Statute contained no such exception, and permitting one would contravene the public policy of the Statute, which was to ensure timely payment of wages in the correct amount. Because employers and employees were free to contract for various pay periods under § 22-2-5-1(a), the lack of a demand or request provision in the Ten-Day Rule of § 22-2-5-1(b) did not significantly impair the constitutional right to contract. Finally, if the term "days" in the Ten-Day Rule referred to calendar and not business days, the due dates for many wage payments would fall on Saturdays or Sundays, when most employees did not work. Based on these practical considerations, the court concluded that the term "days" in the Ten-Day Rule meant business days.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment.

Stephenson v. State


No. 87S00-0106-PD-285


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


864 N.E.2d 1022; 2007 Ind. LEXIS 282



April 26, 2007, Decided  
April 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Stephenson v. State, 2007 Ind. LEXIS 806 (Ind., Sept. 12, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Stephenson v. Indiana, 128 S. Ct. 1871, 170 L. Ed. 2d 751, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3189 (U.S., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Warrick Superior Court, No. 87D02-0210-PC-118. The Honorable Robert R. Aylsworth, Judge.
Stephenson v. State, 742 N.E.2d 463, 2001 Ind. LEXIS 47 (Ind., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition for post-conviction relief after being convicted of burglary, theft, and three murders, and sentenced to death. The Warrick Superior Court (Indiana) denied the inmate's post-conviction petition. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's freestanding claims of error based on wearing a stun belt at trial were available on direct appeal and therefore, foreclosed in post-conviction proceedings. Because appearing in readily visible restraints was inherently prejudicial, if the issue had been raised on appeal, reversal would have been required unless the State proved beyond a reasonable doubt that the error did not affect the result as to either guilt or the penalty. Even if trial counsel's failure to object to the belt or lack of finding of need for any form of restraint fell below prevailing professional norms, the inmate failed to establish a reasonable probability that any such objection would have prevailed; he therefore failed to establish a reasonable probability that the result of either the guilt or the penalty phases would have changed. Because the inmate's claims of newly discovered evidence largely turned on the credibility of various witnesses and were rejected by the post-conviction court, they did not undermine confidence in the inmate's convictions or death sentence. The inmate was not deprived of a fair trial or due process because of the jury's exposure to various extraneous influences.


OUTCOME: The denial of the inmate's post-conviction petition was affirmed.

Voss v. State


No. 49S00-0510-CR-477


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


856 N.E.2d 1211; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 1029



November 22, 2006, Decided  
November 22, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Interlocutory Appeal from the Marion Superior Court No. 49G02-0412-MR-232452. Hon. Judges Robert R. Altice, Jeffrey V. Boles, and Grant W. Hawkins.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with murder. The State made a motion under Ind. R. Crim. P. 12(B) that the judge disqualify himself because of his alleged bias against the death penalty. The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) appointed another judge to decide the recusal motion, and the case was reassigned. Defendant filed an interlocutory appeal, which the present court accepted and assumed expedited appellate jurisdiction to address.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the motion did not support a change of judge. The fact that the judge had ruled that the death penalty statute was unconstitutional did not show bias. Although his decisions as to the death penalty had been reversed, they were supported by reasonable legal argument, and the applicable law was subject at the time to a good-faith difference of opinion. Next, the judge's public comments in a legal newspaper about the death penalty reflected his concern regarding the necessity for extreme care in judicial administration of death penalty cases, but they did not indicate that he would hesitate to impose a sentence of death where appropriate. The judge's representation of defendants in capital crimes before he joined the bench did not provide a rational inference that he would be biased in death penalty cases. Next, it was error to appoint another judge to rule on the motion. The determination in a Rule 12(B) motion, whether the asserted facts supported a rational inference of bias or prejudice, was the type of evaluation that judges did frequently. Finally, the court did not decide whether the judge should disqualify himself under Ind. Code Jud. Conduct Canon 3.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order implicitly granting the State's motion for change of judge and ordering reassignment of the case to a different judge, and the order transferring the case to another judge to rule on the motion. The court remanded the case to the original judge so that he could personally consider whether to disqualify himself under Ind. Code Jud. Conduct Canon 3(E) and for any other proceedings consistent with its opinion.

Prickett v. State


No. 06S04-0509-CR-413


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


856 N.E.2d 1203; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 1027



November 21, 2006, Decided  
November 21, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Boone County Superior Court I, No. 06D01-0110-CF-0107. The Honorable Matthew C. Kincaid, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 06A04-0410-CR-00553.
Prickett v. State, 831 N.E.2d 854, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 1214 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Indiana Court of Appeals, which affirmed his convictions for two counts of child molesting in violation of Ind. Code § 35-42-4-3(a), (b) for having sex with a 13-year-old girl and defendant's sentence of 40 years, restitution under Ind. Code § 35-50-5-3, and registration as a sex offender under Ind. Code § 5-2-12-5(a)(1) (2004). The court granted a transfer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant engaged in sexual intercourse with the victim and touched her breasts while his friends remained in the room and watched. The sentencing court enhanced the sentences from their respective 30-year and four-year presumptive ranges on the following aggravating bases: (1) defendant's probation status; (2) defendant's criminal record; (3) defendant's need of correctional and rehabilitative treatment that was best provided by commitment to a penal facility; and (4) defendant's use of force. On appeal, the court found that the victim's and eyewitnesses' testimony provided sufficient evidence to find defendant guilty. However, the sentencing court erred as it failed to explain sufficiently how a sentence enhancement of ten years would better further the correctional and rehabilitative treatment of defendant than the presumptive term of Ind. Code § 35-50-2-4. While the factors of defendant's criminal history, use of force, and probation status were valid factors to enhance a sentence, none of them were sufficiently weighty to justify a ten-year sentence enhancement. The restitution and sex offender registry did not require a jury trial as Blakely was inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, the order of restitution, and the order to register as a sex offender. The court remanded the action with instructions to impose a sentence of 30 years.

Duncan v. State


No. 79S05-0611-CR-451


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


857 N.E.2d 955; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 1028



November 21, 2006, Decided  
November 21, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Tippecanoe Circuit Court, No. 79C01-0407-MR-01. The Honorable Donald L. Daniel, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 79A05-0505-CR-258.
Duncan v. State, 841 N.E.2d 250, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 2430 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of felony murder, reckless homicide, felony dealing in a schedule II controlled substance, and neglect of a dependent. Defendant was sentenced on the felony murder count to 62 years in prison with 10 years suspended--two years of community corrections, three years of supervised probation, and five years of unsupervised probation. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction and sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant gave a two-year-old child one-fourth of a methadone tablet, and the child died the next day from methadone poisoning. The instant court concluded that sufficient evidence in the record supported defendant's felony murder conviction. The child's ingestion of a controlled substance led directly, if not immediately, to the child's death. Defendant's dealing of the controlled substance was the first step in a chain of events that led to the child's death. The killing occurred during the felony dealing even though the child was not immediately harmed by the pill. The child's death was a foreseeable consequence of defendant's act. However, defendant's prior convictions and charges were neither sufficiently weighty nor similar to the current offense to justify enhancing the sentence. Defendant's conduct was not more serious than the typical murder. Indeed, it was only through a series of stretches that defendant's conduct fell under the murder statute. The appropriate sentence for defendant was the minimum murder sentence of 45 years, which was 15 years more than the presumptive sentence for dealing and 41 more years than the presumptive sentence for reckless homicide.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was revised to 45 years. The instant court summarily affirmed the intermediate appellate court as to issues not addressed in the instant court's opinion.

 

102 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


J.D. v. State


No. 49S04-0508-JV-356


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


853 N.E.2d 945; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 817



September 12, 2006, Decided  
September 12, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion County Superior Court, Juvenile Division, Room 1, No. 49D09-0406-JD-003077. The Honorable James W. Payne, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A04-0410-JV-528.
J. D. v. State, 826 N.E.2d 146, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 678 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, was found to be delinquent. The trial court ordered him to serve a one-year sentence, but refused appellant's request for credit for time served for the 35 days appellant was detained prior to sentencing. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's decision. The present court granted transfer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that Ind. Code § 35-50-6-3, governing credit time, was a procedural statute that should apply to juvenile cases. The court agreed with the court of appeals that Ind. Code § 35-50-6-3 provided substantive rights and was not, therefore, incorporated into the Juvenile Code by Ind. Code § 31-32-1-1. This, however, did not dispose of appellant's claim, as appellant was seeking credit for time served instead of "good time credit" under Ind. Code § 35-50-60-3. The inherent differences between the juvenile delinquency and adult criminal justice systems dictated that a juvenile offender was not entitled to credit for time served in detention prior to sentencing. A key feature of the Juvenile Code was the broad range of alternatives a juvenile court had available once a child was adjudicated as delinquent. Requiring credit for time served would impinge upon this broad authority to fashion dispositional alternatives. Although appellant had been given a "determinate" sentence with a fixed term of one year under Ind. Code § 31-37-19-10, this did not mean credit for time served should be available. Using this provision was one of the trial court's dispositional alternatives.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

103 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Alpha PSI Chptr. v. Auditor of Monroe County


No. 53S00-0508-CV-384


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


849 N.E.2d 1131; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 557



June 29, 2006, Decided  
June 29, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 07, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Monroe Circuit Court, No. 53C04-0309-PL-1678. The Honorable Mary Ellen Diekhoff, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a county auditor and county commissioners, filed a declaratory judgment action against defendant taxpayers, three fraternities. Plaintiffs argued that 2003 Ind. Acts 256, § 44, which extended filing deadlines for the taxpayers, was unconstitutional. The Monroe Circuit Court (Indiana) declared § 44 unconstitutional under Ind. Const. art. IV, §§ 22 and 23. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers each failed to file timely applications for their 2000 and 2001 property tax exemptions. The statute allowed them to apply for their 2000 and 2001 tax exemptions in 2004. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional under Ind. Const. art. IV, § 23; it thus did not reach the question of its constitutionality under Ind. Const. art. IV, § 22. It first held that the statute was a special one. Rather than giving the same retroactive application to all property-owning fraternities or sororities across the state, the statute specified a narrow group within the larger class, fraternities connected with a specific university that had been previously granted property tax exemptions but which had paid property tax for two specified years because of their failure to file an exemption. Next, in determining whether a special law was unconstitutional, a court was to look at whether a class was unique and whether that uniqueness justified differential treatment. The class here was not unique. The taxpayers' problems were not different from those faced by land-owning fraternities and sororities throughout the state. The taxpayers' oversight did not justify special treatment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

104 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Hardister v. State


No. 49S05-0507-CR-319


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


849 N.E.2d 563; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 552



June 28, 2006, Decided  
June 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 07, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49G20-0012-CF-221197. The Honorable Michael Jensen, Magistrate. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A05-0310-CR-535.
Hardister v. State, 821 N.E.2d 912, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 162 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed defendant's convictions and sentence after the trial court entered judgments on a jury's verdict finding him guilty of Class A felony dealing cocaine, unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon as a Class B felony, obstruction of justice as a Class D felony, and felony possession of cocaine as Class A felony based on certain factors. He was then sentenced to 68 years in prison. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Two police officers responded to an anonymous tip that persons with guns were "cooking drugs" at a certain location. The officers knocked on the front door. The officers then saw two men fleeing to the rear of the residence. The officers went to the back of the residence; one officer peered through a rear window. That officer saw defendant pouring a white powder substance down a drain. Three people who had been inside gained access to the roof, and one started tossing away plastic bags of white powder. The officers entered the house and arrested everyone inside. Laboratory tests confirmed that the bags tossed contained cocaine. A loaded handgun and shotgun were recovered, as well as drug distribution items. Defendant was convicted on multiple counts related to the events and sentenced to 68 years in prison; the appellate court reversed. On further review, the state supreme court found that evidence was properly admitted since defendant's search and seizure rights had not been violated, that two convictions had to be reversed since they were not proven by sufficient evidence, that the remaining convictions were affirmed, and that the proper sentence was 53 years.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgments of conviction and sentences for Class C felony possession of cocaine and a firearm, and Class B felony possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon, were reversed. The remainder of defendant's convictions were affirmed. Too, defendant's sentence was revised to 53 years in prison.

 

105 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Cantrell v. Morris


No. 94S00-0505-CQ-243


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


849 N.E.2d 488; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 514



June 21, 2006, Decided  
June 21, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 26, 2006.
Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by, Motion denied by Cantrell v. Morris, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 90960 (N.D. Ind., Dec. 14, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Certified Question from the United States District Court, Northern District of Indiana, No. 2:04-CV-364-PPS-APR. The Honorable Andrew P. Rodovich, Judge.
Cantrell v. Morris, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 37388 (N.D. Ind., Sept. 14, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a suit that plaintiff public defender brought against defendant judge for wrongful discharge, the United States District Court, Northern District of Indiana, certified a question for clarification as to whether Ind. Const. art. I, § 9, restricted government officials in dealing with political activity or affiliations of their public employees and whether damages were available if such an employee's free speech rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: The judge fired the defender for supporting the candidacy of her opponent. In answering the certified question, the court did not answer it as framed but responded by ruling that to the extent that tort doctrines gave a civil damage remedy to a public employee terminated for political activity or affiliation in violation of Ind. Const. art. I, § 9, a wrongful discharge claim was governed by the Indiana Tort Claims Act (ITCA). Moreover, the court ruled that the Indiana Constitution did not give rise to any such claim and did not prevent application of the ITCA. In so ruling, the court examined federal and state law applicable to such a situation, concluding that there were significant differences between state and federal laws on the subject and that because there was an available remedy under the ITCA, there was no need to consider whether a private right of action or a general civil right to damages under the Indiana Constitution existed. Thus, the court ruled that a terminated employee had no private right to damages under Ind. Const. art. I, § 9. The court also examined the application of qualified immunity, including immunity for a discretionary act in Ind. Code § 34-13-3-3(7).


OUTCOME: The court stated that there was no private right of action under the state constitution and that the cause of action arose under the Indiana Tort Claims Act.


 

106 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Ledbetter v. Hunter


No. 49S02-0412-CV-501


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


842 N.E.2d 810; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 138



February 22, 2006, Decided  
February 22, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected February 23, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-9409-CT-384. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0309-CV-770.
Ledbetter v. Hunter, 810 N.E.2d 1095, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 1199 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the mother of an injured decedent, sued appellees, two doctors and a hospital, for medical negligence. The trial court dismissed the case as time-barred. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed, finding that Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b), the Indiana Medical Malpractice Act's statute of limitations regarding claims of minors, violated the Privileges and Immunities Clause, Ind. Const. art. I, § 23. Appellees sought review.


OVERVIEW: The decedent's birth complications caused her serious and permanent physical and mental injuries. For religious reasons, her mother did not assert a malpractice claim against appellees. Less than two years after her 18th birthday, the decedent sued defendants. She died during the pendency of the appeal, and her mother was substituted as appellant. Under Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b), a person under the age of six had until his or her eight birthday to file a malpractice suit. The mother argued that § 34-18-7-1(b) violated Ind. Const. art. I, § 23, because victims of torts other than malpractice had until two years after the age of majority to file suit. The high court disagreed. The classification of those entitled to legal disability by age and type of claim bore a fair and substantial relationship to the purpose of the Indiana Medical Malpractice Act: to protect the health of Indiana citizens by preventing a reduction of health care services. The mother's attempt to demonstrate that there was a lack of substantial evidence supporting this legislative rationale did not affirmatively establish that the rationale was unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing the action.

 

107 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Bryant v. State


No. 48S04-0602-CR-00040


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


841 N.E.2d 1154; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 101



February 8, 2006, Decided  
February 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Madison Circuit Court, No. 48C01-0107-FB-195. The Honorable Fredrick R. Spencer, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 48A04-0403-CR-00132.
Bryant v. State, 821 N.E.2d 45, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 11 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with and found guilty of armed robbery and criminal confinement, as class B felonies. His two 20-year sentences were to be served consecutively. The trial court enhanced his sentence based on a finding that defendant "lay in wait" and on defendant's prior criminal convictions. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised two issues: (1) whether the sentences were properly enhanced; and (2) whether the consecutive sentences were properly imposed. He argued that his sentences were unconstitutional because the enhancements exceeded the presumptive sentence established by the legislature and were based upon aggravating factors not found by a jury. The first aggravator could not support the sentencing enhancement because it increased the sentence beyond the statutory maximum and was not found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. That aggravator was precisely the sort of "fact" with which Blakely concerned itself. Although the second aggravator, defendant's criminal history, did not need to be submitted to a jury, the extent and complete nature of defendant's criminal history was not clearly laid out in either the trial record or the record on appeal. In the absence of a presentencing report, or a more complete explanation of defendant's criminal history and its relationship to the instant offense, the Supreme Court of Indiana could not sustain the sentencing enhancements. However, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in imposing consecutive sentences.


OUTCOME: The sentencing enhancement was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for new sentencing, either through a clearer explanation of defendant's criminal history or, should the State elect, through the intervention of a jury. The imposition of the consecutive sentences, and the judgment was otherwise affirmed.

Horseman v. Keller


No. 49S00-0501-CV-17


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


841 N.E.2d 164; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 57



January 31, 2006, Decided  
January 31, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, Civil Division 10, No. 49D10-0311-MI-001998. The Honorable David Dreyer, Judge. On Direct Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court, Civil Division (Indiana), affirmed a decision of the court-appointed Recount Commission that concluded that a certain person was the winner of a specific city council election. In making that ruling, the trial court also declared an absentee voting statute, Ind. Code § 3-12-1-13, to be unconstitutional. Appellant losing candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: During a general election, the municipal election included a contest for a city council seat. On the ballot were the losing candidate, appellee winning candidate, and one other candidate. The losing candidate and winning candidate received most of the votes. The initial results showed the winning candidate ahead by just a few votes. The losing candidate's petitioned for a recount. The Recount Commission investigated and determined that the winning candidate had prevailed. Part of its conclusion was based on its finding that two absentee ballots containing votes for the losing candidate were correctly rejected pursuant to Ind. Code § 3-12-1-13 based on certain irregularities apparently due to human error. The trial court affirmed the Recount Commission's decision that found the winning candidate prevailed; in doing so, it also declared that the absentee voting statute, Ind. Code § 3-12-1-13, violated Ind. Const. art. I, § 23. On further review, the state supreme court found that the trial court's judgment that the Recount Commission's findings of voter intent were non-appealable findings of fact was correct, but also found that Ind. Code § 3-12-1-13 was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that found the Recount Commission's findings of voter intent were non-appealable findings of fact was affirmed. However, the trial court's judgment declaring the absentee voting statute to be unconstitutional was reversed.

City of Vincennes v. Emmons


No. 42S02-0504-CV-131


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


841 N.E.2d 155; 2006 Ind. LEXIS 54



January 25, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by City of Vincennes v. Emmons, 2006 Ind. LEXIS 410 (Ind., May 11, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Knox Superior Court, Nos. 42D02-0006-OV-130; 42D02-0006-OV-135; and 42D02-0006-OV-132. The Honorable Robert L. Arthur, Special Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 42A02-0402-CV-193.
City of Vincennes v. Emmons, 817 N.E.2d 291, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 2227 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a city, challenged the judgment of the Indiana Court of Appeals, which affirmed a trial court's dismissal of the city's civil suit against appellee landlords, which sought to collect unpaid annual registration fees for regulation and inspection of residential rental units pursuant to the city's rental housing code.


OVERVIEW: The landlords admitted that the code called for payment of registration fees but contended that the entire code was unconstitutional because its provision for inspection of rental units violated the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution as authorizing or requiring unreasonable searches. The trial court agreed and dismissed the city's complaint and the lower court affirmed, holding that the landlords had standing to raise the constitutionality of the inspection provision of the code, that the inspection provision violated the Fourth Amendment, and that since the inspection provision was not severable, the entire code was unconstitutional. The court agreed with the lower courts that the landlords had standing to challenge the code provision but did not concur that a Fourth Amendment violation occurred. To the contrary, the court held that because the landlords did not live in the rental units at issue, they had no reasonable expectation of privacy regarding the inspection of the units. As such, the landlords had no substantial protectable interest at stake.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court dismissing the city's civil action.

Booth v. Wiley


No. 02S03-0402-CV-95


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


839 N.E.2d 1168; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 1158



December 30, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D01-0107-CT-260. The Honorable Nancy Eshcoff Boyer, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 02A03-0210-CV-355.
Booth v. Wiley, 793 N.E.2d 1104, 2003 Ind. App. LEXIS 1524 (Ind. Ct. App., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a husband and wife, appealed the decision of the Allen Superior Court (Indiana), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, two doctors, and their professional corporation doing business as a cataract and laser eye institute. Plaintiffs had brought suit against defendants asserting medical malpractice. The court granted transfer of the case.


OVERVIEW: The husband, already suffering from preexisting cataracts and glaucoma, consulted one of the two doctors, who referred the husband to the other doctor for "Lasik" surgery. Lasik surgery was performed on both eyes as was cataract surgery on the right eye. After various surgeries, the husband was left nearly blind in his right eye. During a consultation with a third doctor over two years later, that doctor told plaintiffs that Lasik surgery should not have been performed because of the preexisting cataracts and glaucoma. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendants upon concluding that plaintiffs' action was barred by the two-year medical malpractice statute of limitations set forth in Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b). In their appeal, plaintiffs contended that, because the evidence showed that they did not discover the alleged malpractice until more than two years after it occurred, and they initiated their action within eight months thereafter, the trial court erred in finding their action time-barred, which was reasonable under caselaw. The court agreed and held that plaintiffs did not reasonably suspect medical malpractice until the consultation with the third doctor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting defendants' motions for summary judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Clinic for Women, Inc. v. Brizzi


No. 49S05-0501-CV-31


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


837 N.E.2d 973; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 1034



November 23, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D07-0303-PL-558. The Honorable Gerald Zore, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A05-0305-CV-259.
Clinic for Women, Inc. v. Brizzi, 814 N.E.2d 1042, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 1829 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted transfer, thereby vacating a judgment of the Indiana Court of Appeals that affirmed a trial court's dismissal of plaintiff abortion providers' challenges to Ind. Code § 16-34-2-1.1 based on several Indiana constitutional provisions, but remanded for a determination as to whether enforcement by defendant state official would violate a woman's natural rights in violation of Ind. Const. art. I, § 1.


OVERVIEW: After dismissal of their federal lawsuit arguing that Ind. Code § 16-34-2-1.1 materially burdened a woman's right to make reproductive decisions, on grounds that the mere fact of two visits to the doctor 18 hours apart did not violate the federal right to privacy, the providers filed a second lawsuit, arguing most prominently that the statute unduly burdened a core constitutional value protected by Ind. Const. art. I, § 1. The court noted that it was not bound by the federal material burden analysis in Casey when examining Indiana constitutional claims. Nonetheless, and assuming that a woman's right to choose was a core constitutional value in Indiana, it held that the undue burden standard it used was nearly identical to the federal standard. It went on to find that the providers had not met their very heavy burden of showing, as was always necessary in a prospective challenge to the facial validity of a statute, that there were no circumstances in which it could be applied without an undue burden. There was a medical necessity exception, and women in a great hurry, who might have to seek an abortion elsewhere, faced only what was essentially a fairly minimal economic burden.


OUTCOME: Having previously vacated the intermediate judgment, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment of dismissal for failure to state a claim.

Metro. Dev. Comm'n v. Pinnacle Media, LLC


No. 49S05-0511-CV-510


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


836 N.E.2d 422; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 985



November 3, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing granted by Metro. Dev. Comm'n v. Pinnacle Media, 2006 Ind. LEXIS 367 (Ind., May 3, 2006)
Reconsideration granted by, Adhered to Metro. Dev. Comm'n v. Pinnacle Media, LLC, 846 N.E.2d 654, 2006 Ind. LEXIS 360 (Ind., May 3, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court, No. 49D02-0201-PL-000078. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A05-0309-CV-465.
Metro. Dev. Comm'n v. Pinnacle Media, L.L.C., 811 N.E.2d 404, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 1259 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the trial court reversed and remanded matter to the trial court with instructions to grant the City's motion for summary judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee LLC sued appellant city, seeking a declaration that an amendment to the city's zoning ordinance was inapplicable to 10 permits to erect billboards, and that the city's stop work order was unenforceable. The trial court denied the city's summary judgment motion and granted summary judgment for the LLC. The Court of Appeals (Indiana) affirmed the determination that the amendment was inapplicable to the 10 permits. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: When the LLC set out to erect the 10 billboards, there was no location permit required by the city. The LLC then engaged in negotiations with the state agency to obtained the permits needed to erect the billboards. In the interim, however, the city reconsidered its policy and enacted the amendment which assigned zoning classifications to the areas in which the LLC planned on erecting the billboards. Nearly a year later, the LLC received the state permits, and began construction. The appellate court held that this was a nonconforming use case, and the amendment was subject to any vested rights in the property of the LLC acquired before enactment of the amendment. The issue was whether, at the time of the change in the zoning ordinance, construction had proceeded on the project to the point that the LLC had a vested interest. No construction of any kind had proceeded on the 10 billboards as the date the ordinance change was officially proposed, or even the date it was enacted. There was no basis in law or logic for the LLC's proposition that its filing of applications for permits with the State immunized it from the city's zoning change.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to grant the city's motion for summary judgment.

Pruitt v. State


No. 15S00-0109-DP-393


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


834 N.E.2d 90; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 822



September 13, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Pruitt v. State, 2005 Ind. LEXIS 1074 (Ind., Nov. 22, 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pruitt v. Indiana, 548 U.S. 910, 126 S. Ct. 2936, 165 L. Ed. 2d 962, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5015 (2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Pruitt v. State, 903 N.E.2d 899, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 338 (Ind., 2009)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability granted, in part, Certificate of appealability denied, in part Pruitt v. Wilson, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 142162 (N.D. Ind., Oct. 2, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Dearborn Circuit Court, No. 15C01-0109-CF-00054. The Honorable James D. Humphrey, Judge. On Direct Appeal.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought direct review as of right of a judgment of the Dearborn Circuit Court (Indiana) imposing the death penalty on a jury recommendation after he was found guilty of murdering a law enforcement officer and related offenses. His primary argument was that he should have been found mentally retarded within the meaning of Ind. Code § 35-36-9-2 (2004) and ineligible for the death penalty, but he raised many other constitutional issues.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death under a jury recommendation pursuant to Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9 (2004), after the jury weighed aggravators and mitigators. The court had previously found that statutory scheme did not violate the Sixth Amendment, and it adhered to its earlier decisions. Defendant offered evidence of mental retardation, arguing that it should preclude his execution. The court agreed with defendant that Ind. Code § 35-36-9-4 (2004) improperly required him to establish mental retardation by clear and convincing instead of a preponderance of the evidence, but since the trial court remarked that defendant had not met even the later standard, reversal was not required. Ind. Code § 35-36-9-2 (2004) required defendant to show retardation both in terms of intelligence and in terms of adaptive abilities. Although the trial court had applied too high a standard in determining the latter, there was no infirmity in its finding, based on clinical standards recognized in a national consensus, that defendant had not established low intelligence. Defendant's other arguments, primarily focused on admissibility of evidence and sentencing procedures, were all meritless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and upheld, with one exception, Indiana's statutory scheme involving determination of mental retardation in a death penalty context.

SMDfund, Inc. v. Fort Wayne-Allen County Airport Auth.


No. 02S00-0409-CV-410


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


831 N.E.2d 725; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 673



August 2, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Tocci v. Fort Wayne-Allen County Airport Auth., 546 U.S. 1093, 126 S. Ct. 1051, 163 L. Ed. 2d 859, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 80 (U.S., Jan. 9, 2006)

DISPOSITION: Judgment of the trial court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, two citizens, appealed the decision of the Allen Circuit Court (Indiana), which granted a motion for summary judgment filed by defendants, a city, a county, and its airport authority. The citizens had filed a complaint seeking a declaratory judgment that the authority had no legal control over an airfield and that the statute creating the authority, Ind. Code § 8-22-3-1.1 (1985), was unconstitutional special legislation.


OVERVIEW: The suit arose over the citizens' concerns that an airfield was going to be closed. The citizens sought a declaration that the city, not the authority, held legal title to the airfield. The citizens also had sought injunctive relief to prevent the authority from closing, altering, or destroying the airfield. The trial court granted the summary judgment to defendants based on the claims being barred by the statute of limitations and by the equitable doctrine of laches. The court upheld the judgment in favor of defendants, finding that 17 years had passed since the authority had been created. The court found that the citizens did not challenge the constitutionality of the substance of the authority statute but rather the procedure by which the authority statute was passed, namely allegedly establishing special legislation. The court noted that the public expenditure in creating the authority exceeded $ 44 million and that various leases, contracts, bonds, and other obligations were created, some of which extended for 68 years into the future.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

115 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Celebration Fireworks, Inc.


No. 18S02-0501-CV-4


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


829 N.E.2d 979; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 600



June 30, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Delaware Circuit Court No. 3, No. 18D01-9501-CP-06. The Honorable Robert L. Barnet, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 18A02-0305-CV-437.
Boatwright v. Celebration Fireworks, Inc., 810 N.E.2d 766, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 1155 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment reversed. Matter to the trial court with instructions to dismiss Celebration's complaint for lack of subject matter jurisdiction remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee fireworks company sued appellants marshal and State to prevent collection of fees on fireworks sellers, asserting that Ind. Code § 22-11-14-5 only required it to obtain one Certificate of Compliance for all its wholesale locations and seeking a refund of fees paid in previous years. The trial court entered judgment for the fireworks company and the Court of Appeals (Indiana) affirmed. The marshal and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The fireworks company had 96 locations, and for four years, obtained a Certificate of Compliance for each separate location. The next year, however, the fireworks company tendered only one application and fee for its central warehouse, and then, and without seeking administrative review, filed the complaint. The marshal and the State argued that the fireworks company failed to exhaust its administrative remedies. The intermediate appellate court held that exhaustion of remedies was not required in this case because compliance would have been futile, and there was doubt as to the availability of an administrative remedy. The appellate court ruled that there was no question of the marshal's legal authority to license fireworks wholesalers; the question was whether each of the individual outlets selling fireworks was itself a wholesaler. This was not a question of law, but a question of fact properly resolved through the administrative process. The mere fact than an administrative agency might refuse to provide the relief requested did not amount to futility. The failure to exhaust administrative remedies deprived the trial court of subject matter jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

 

116 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Monroe Guar. Ins. Co. v. Magwerks Corp.


No. 49S02-0402-CV-81


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


829 N.E.2d 968; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 599



June 29, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Marion Superior Court No. 7, No. 49D07-9902-CP-228. The Honorable Gerald S. Zore, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 49A02-0208-CV-622.
Monroe Guar. Ins. Co. v. Magwerks Corp., 796 N.E.2d 326, 2003 Ind. App. LEXIS 1783 (Ind. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the trial court reversed and cause remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured sued defendant insurer for breach of contract and sought punitive damages for the insurer's alleged lack of good faith and fair dealing. The trial court granted the insured summary judgment on its breach of contract claim, and a jury awarded it punitive damages. The Indiana Court of Appeals reversed the summary judgment and remanded for a new trial, in which punitive damages could not be awarded. The insured appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a period of heavy rain and snow, sections of the roof of the insured's building fell. The primary issue in dispute at trial was whether the insured's loss was due to a "collapse" of the roof, in which case it was covered. The court of appeals adopted the modern definition of "collapse"; under that definition, there was a genuine fact issue as to whether there was a collapse. The supreme court agreed with both holdings. Factual disputes as to whether there was a "substantial impairment of the structural integrity" of the building or any part of it precluded summary judgment. However, the court of appeals erred in ruling that the parties' good faith dispute concerning coverage barred the insured's punitive damages claim for bad faith. The insured suggested that the insurer initially acknowledged that the roof collapsed and that its asserted denial of coverage based on its understanding of the meaning of the term collapse was a manufactured excuse appearing for the first time its answer to the insured's complaint. If true, this allegation would allow a jury to find the insurer acted in bad faith.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated that portion of the court of appeals opinion holding that the insurer could not be liable to pay punitive damages, but affirmed its opinion in all other respects.

 

117 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Advantage Home Health Care, Inc. v. Ind. State Dep't of Health


No. 18S02-0405-CV-198


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


829 N.E.2d 499; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 557



June 22, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Delaware Circuit Court, No. 18C01-0109-CP-547. The Honorable Marianne Vorhees, Master Commissioner. The Honorable Wayne Lennington, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 18A02-0211-CV-928.
Advantage Home Health Care, Inc. v. Ind. State Dep't of Health, 792 N.E.2d 914, 2003 Ind. App. LEXIS 1412 (Ind. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted transfer with regard to the decision of the Indiana Court of Appeals, which held that a survey and statement of deficiencies issued by appellee, the Indiana Department of Health, constituted an order that was appealable. Appellant home health agency had filed a complaint for a declaratory judgment seeking to reverse the Department's position that the survey was not an order subject to review.


OVERVIEW: The Department was responsible for ensuring that the agency, and other providers, complied with various state statutes and regulations concerning the operation of home health agencies. The Department completed a survey of the agency after receiving a complaint that a nurse employed by the agency struck a special needs child in the mouth. Three violations of the state rules were found, along with 26 federal violations. The Department sent a letter of deficiency. The agency filed a plan of correction but failed to follow through with seeking administrative review of the survey and, instead, brought the declaratory judgment action. The Department informed the agency that the survey did not constitute a final order and was not subject to judicial review under the Administrative Orders and Procedures Act, Ind. Code Ann. §§ 4-21.5-1-1 to -7-9 (2002). The court agreed that the survey and letter did not constitute a final order subject to judicial review and that, therefore, the trial court was correct in granting summary judgment to the Department.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court and affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

118 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Cox v. Paul


No. 71S03-0409-CV-417


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


828 N.E.2d 907; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 575



June 14, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the St. Joseph Circuit Court, No. 71C01-9801-CP-000135. The Honorable Terry A. Crone, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 71A03-0303-CV-92.
Cox v. Paul, 805 N.E.2d 901, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 587 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004).



DISPOSITION: Order of the trial court on the parties' cross motions for partial summary judgment vacated. Case remanded with instructions to grant the plaintiffs' motion for partial summary judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a patient and her husband, appealed the order from the St. Joseph Circuit Court (Indiana), which denied their motion for partial summary judgment on the issue of liability in their medical malpractice suit against defendant oral surgeon. The court granted a transfer of the case after the court of appeals reversed the judgment from the trial court.


OVERVIEW: In 1983, the patient consulted the surgeon about problems in her temporomandibular joints, which connect the jaw to the skull. In 1984, he performed a surgical replacement of her joints using Vitek dental implants. In 1991, the Food and Drug Administration announced a recall of Vitek implants. Although he instructed his staff to search his patient charts and identify patients who had received implants and warn them of the dangers, the patient was not identified until 1996. Plaintiffs then sued the surgeon, alleging that he breached his duty to warn the patient. The trial court denied their motion for summary judgment on the issue of liability. On review, the court ruled in favor of plaintiffs, finding that an analysis similar to res ipsa loquitur should apply and that a showing that no communication was received was sufficient to shift the burden to the doctor to explain what steps he took to notify the patient or why no steps were taken. As there was no evidence of any attempt to notify the patient and no claim of an office fire or other intervening event, the court found that there was no room for any explanation other than negligence attributable to the surgeon or his staff.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order from the trial court and remanded with instructions to grant plaintiffs' motion for partial summary judgment.

Johnson v. State


Supreme Court Cause No. 48S00-0505-SD-192


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


827 N.E.2d 547; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 449



May 16, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Madison Superior Court Cause No. 3SCR85-71.
Johnson v. State, 584 N.E.2d 1092, 1992 Ind. LEXIS 14 (Ind., 1992).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder and arson. The jury unanimously recommended the death penalty. Those convictions and sentence were affirmed in the several proceedings that followed, and defendant received all of the review to which he was entitled as a matter of right. Defendant subsequently tendered a successive post-conviction relief petition and other papers requesting permission to litigate additional collateral claims in state court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant admitted breaking into the victim's house, taking some of her belongings, stepping on her, and starting a fire. The victim had died, not from the fire or the smoke, but from severe blunt force trauma consistent with being repeatedly hit by a broom handle and kicked. The appellate court held defendant had already availed himself of Ind. R.P. Post-Conviction Remedies 1. Defendant contended the State should have disclosed that it had another suspect in the murder. The appellate court held that since defendant admitted participating in the crimes, any information about whether the other suspect was involved would have been known to defendant before trial. Also, despite defendant's already knowing whether the other suspect or anyone else had been an accomplice, defendant waited until his convictions and sentence had been affirmed on direct appeal to make those assertions publicly. He had already litigated this claim in state courts and had enjoyed thorough consideration by the Supreme Court of Indiana on both direct appeal and collateral attack. No matter the results of any DNA test, defendant made no argument that the results would be exculpatory.


OUTCOME: The court did not authorize the filing of a successive petition for post-conviction relief.

 

120 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Estes v. State


No. 48S05-0505-CR-208


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


827 N.E.2d 27; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 441



May 10, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Madison Circuit Court, No. 48C01-0308-FA-248, The Honorable Fredrick R. Spencer, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 48A05-0405-CR-262.
Estes v. State, 820 N.E.2d 180, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 2505 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to 14 counts of child molesting and sexual misconduct with a minor and one count of intimidation. The trial court sentenced him to 267 years in jail, which sentence was affirmed by the Indiana Court of Appeals. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The charges arose out of a series of molestations of two victims over a period of years. There was no plea agreement as to the sentence or the amount of jail time. The trial court identified six aggravating circumstances. As to mitigating circumstances, the trial court considered defendant's expression of remorse, his minimal criminal history, and his guilty plea. The trial court rejected as mitigating circumstances defendant's own childhood abuse and his depression. The appellate court found that it did not need to address defendant's arguments on whether the trial court should have submitted sentencing facts finding to a jury since the trial court might have imposed essentially the same 267 years of jail time by ordering other sentences served consecutively. A trial court's authority to order consecutive sentences was not affected by Blakely. However, the 267-year sentence was well outside the typical range of sentences imposed for child molesting. There were mitigating circumstances. Defendant had no substantial criminal history, and he expressed remorse. His prompt admissions and guilty plea saved judicial resources and spared the victims from trial.


OUTCOME: Transfer was granted, and the case was remanded with directions for the trial court to rearrange the sentence to four consecutive standard terms for a class A felony, a total of 120 years, and to impose the other sentences concurrently.

 

121 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Barker


No. 49S00-0308-DP-392


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


826 N.E.2d 648; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 402



May 4, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Barker v. Indiana, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 8486 (U.S., Nov. 14, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Barker, 809 N.E.2d 312, 2004 Ind. LEXIS 477 (Ind., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of, inter alia, two counts of murder. The trial court then dismissed the State's request for the death penalty and ordered a sentencing proceeding where a term of years was the only question. On appeal, the court reversed that order, and remanded the case for reinstatement of the death penalty request. Defendant requested a rehearing.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(f) was unconstitutional because it did not require a penalty phase jury to find that mitigating circumstances outweighed aggravating circumstances beyond a reasonable doubt. Because § 35-50-2-9(f) permitted a death sentence to be imposed by a judge alone in cases where the jury was not able to reach a sentencing decision, defendant claimed that it was facially unconstitutional under Ind. Const. art. 1, § 19. The court held that Ind. Const. art. 1, § 19 did not apply to sentencing proceedings and thus was inapplicable in capital cases at the penalty phase. Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(f) was not unconstitutional as written. The court declined to extend the right of jury trial provided in Ind. Const. art. 1, § 19 to the determination of "weighing," which was not a "fact" necessarily requiring a jury decision under Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(f).


OUTCOME: The court reached the same conclusion as its previous opinion. The trial court's order finding that Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9 was unconstitutional and dismissing the State's request for the death penalty was reversed. The case was remanded for reinstatement of the State's death penalty request and for penalty phase proceedings as previously ordered.

 

122 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. State


No. 64S03-0406-CR-284


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


825 N.E.2d 783; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 385



April 20, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Smith v. State, 2010 Ind. App. LEXIS 775 (Ind. Ct. App., Apr. 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Porter Superior Court, No. 64D01-0210-FB-9017. The Honorable Roger V. Bradford, Judge. On Petition to Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 64A03-0306-CR-204.
Smith v. State, 804 N.E.2d 1246, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 446 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of attempted rape, criminal confinement, intimidation, and battery. Pursuant to the Indiana Repeat Sexual Offender Statute, Ind. Code § 35-50-2-14 (2004), the trial court found him to be a "repeat sexual offender" and increased his sentence by 10 years. The Indiana Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction but vacated the sentence, finding that § 35-50-2-14 violated the Indiana Constitution. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant locked the victim in his car, grabbed her by the neck, choked her, and demanded that she perform a sex act. She managed to escape. Defendant argued that the trial court's failure to allow the jury to find his status as a non-habitual offender directly contradicted Seay and violated Ind. Const. art. I, § 19. The high court disagreed. The statute at issue in Seay specifically assigned to the jury the duty to determine the status of a habitual offender, whereas, in enacting Ind. Code § 35-50-2-14, the legislature structured an "automatic" sentencing enhancement scheme without a jury trial on the status determination. Therefore, there was no violation of defendant's right under Ind. Const. art. I, § 19 to have a jury determine the law and the facts. As to the U.S. Constitution, the fact of a prior conviction was an exception to the Apprendi rule that any fact that increased the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum had to be submitted to a jury. The Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments did not require a jury determination of the fact of a prior conviction in order to impose a recidivist sentencing enhancement.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Houser v. State


No. 57S00-0310-CR-433


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


823 N.E.2d 693; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 198



March 10, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Noble Superior Court 1, No. 57D01-0010-CF-20. The Honorable Steven S. Spindler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentences affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Noble Superior Court (Indiana), which convicted defendant of knowing or intentional murder in violation of Ind. Code § 35-42-1-1 and burglary causing death in violation of Ind. Code § 35-43-2-1(2). Due to the aggravating circumstances found, defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole (LWOP) pursuant to Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9(b)(1)(B) (2004).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction arose from the murder of an elderly woman during the course of burglarizing her home. He challenged his convictions on grounds of trial court error in the admission of certain evidence, the constitutionality of the statute under which he was sentenced, and the severity of the sentence itself. The court found that the admission of testimony from defendant's girlfriend that he listened frequently to a heavy metal song regarding night prowling was harmless error because any probable impact on the jury affected no substantial right of defendant in light of the other evidence of guilt in the case. The court had previously addressed and rejected the constitutional claims raised by defendant with regard to his LWOP sentence, and the court held that the sentence imposed pursuant to § 35-50-2-9(b)(1)(B) was appropriate given the relative weight of the charged aggravating circumstance and the mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Smylie v. State


No. 41S01-0409-CR-408


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


823 N.E.2d 679; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 199



March 9, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Smylie v. Ind., 2005 U.S. LEXIS 7865 (U.S., Oct. 31, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Johnson Superior Court, No. 41D01-0207-FC-15. On Petition To Transfer from the Indiana Court of Appeals, No. 41A01-0309-CR-339. The Honorable Kevin Barton, Judge.
Smylie v. State, 807 N.E.2d 809, 2004 Ind. App. LEXIS 691 (Ind. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Part of Smylie's sentence that enhances the standard penalty reversed; remanded for new sentencing hearing in which the State may elect to prove adequate aggravating circumstances before a jury or accept the statutory fixed term. Order for consecutive sentences affirmed. Decision of Court of Appeals superseded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Johnson Superior Court (Indiana), which sentenced him to consecutive two-year terms following his guilty plea to two counts of child solicitation and which suspended six months of the sentences for a total sentence of three and one-half years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to child solicitation, which was a class D felony that carried a fixed term of one and a half years. The trial court increased defendant's sentence beyond the fixed term under the authority of Ind. Code § 35-50-2-7 (2004) after it found factors in aggravation and in mitigation. Defendant appealed, challenging the constitutionality of the sentence under the Blakely doctrine. The court found, first, that Indiana's sentencing scheme was unconstitutional because it allowed aggravating factors to be determined by a judge, not a jury, and it allowed such factors to be used to enhance a sentence beyond the statutory maximum. U.S. Const. amend. VI required a jury finding on such enhancing factors. However, consecutive sentencing under Ind. Code § 35-50-1-2(c) (2004) that was premised on the trial court's discretionary findings on certain factors was constitutional. The court rejected the State's argument that the Blakely doctrine claim was unpreserved since the doctrine was a new rule of constitutional procedure that applied retroactively to defendant's case. Also, the failure to object on Blakely grounds before the lower court did not waive or forfeit the issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of defendant's sentencing judgment that enhanced the standard penalty for the crime of child solicitation and remanded for a new sentencing hearing where the State could elect either to prove aggravating circumstances before a jury or to accept the statutory fixed term. The court affirmed that part of the sentencing judgment that ordered consecutive sentences.

Laux v. State


No. 27S00-0303-CR-104


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


821 N.E.2d 816; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 93



February 2, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Laux v. State, 2013 Ind. App. LEXIS 81 (Ind. Ct. App., Feb. 20, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Grant Superior Court, No. 27D01-0202-MR-28. The Honorable Gary L. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: No-contact order vacated, affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a Grant Superior Court (Indiana) judgment on jury verdict sentencing him to life without parole pursuant to the death penalty statute, Ind. Code § 35-50-2-9, and after he was found guilty of murder, felony murder, and burglary in the killing of his former wife. He challenged an order that he have no contact with his former in-laws or his children and argued that his sentences violated double jeopardy guarantees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a person with no criminal history, in a rage at his ex-wife, left his children sleeping at his house, crept into her house, and murdered her. After briefly noting that the Indiana process of weighing aggravators and mitigators to determine available sentencing ranges did not violate the right to trial by jury, the court held that while the trial court could have imposed a no-contact order, for example, as a condition of a suspended sentence, it was not an authorized sentence under the murder or the burglary statute. The sentence did not violate double jeopardy guarantees either, since defendant's felony murder conviction merged into the murder conviction before sentencing and since the separate weighing of sentencing factors under the death penalty statute did not amount to a separate prosecution. Finally, the court refused to grant relief from the sentence on grounds of manifest unreasonableness. Despite defendant's lack of criminal history, his fine work ethic, his education, and his remorse, the trial court appropriately exercised its discretion in finding that the single aggravator of breaking into the wife's house and brutally killing her outweighed any mitigators.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the no-contact order but affirmed the judgment in all other respects.

Wallace v. State


Supreme Court case no. 84S00-0412-SD-502


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


820 N.E.2d 1261; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 35



January 13, 2005, Decided  
January 13, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Vigo Circuit Court case no. C-CR-80-9.
Wallace v. Davis, 362 F.3d 914, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 5691 (7th Cir. Ind., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Request denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a request for permission to file a successive petition for post-conviction relief after he was convicted on four counts of murder, was sentenced to death, and had his convictions and sentenced reviewed numerous times in the state and federal courts as a matter of right. The state supreme court then considered that request.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of four counts of murder and sentenced to death on the unanimous recommendation of a jury. Since then, he had the convictions and sentence reviewed by a state trial court in two post-conviction proceedings, by the state supreme court three times on appeal, and at all three levels of the federal judicial system. He then filed a petition that requested permission to seek further review in state court. He conceded that the courts had already considered and rejected the claims he presented. The state supreme court considered the successive petition for post-conviction relief and declined to authorize the filing of it. It found that the standard for authorizing consideration of the successive petition was whether the petitioner had established a reasonable possibility that the petitioner was entitled to post-conviction relief. Since the petition filed by petitioner did not establish a reasonable possibility that he was entitled to relief, the state supreme court did not find that any reason existed to consider the successive petition.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's request to seek further review in state court of his four murder convictions and sentence of death was denied, as the state supreme court declined to authorize the filing of his successive petition for post-conviction relief.

State ex rel. Att'y Gen. v. Lake Superior Court


No. 45S00-0405-OR-204, No. 45S00-0405-CV-224


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


820 N.E.2d 1240; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 32



January 13, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State ex rel. AG of Ind. v. Superior Court, 2005 Ind. LEXIS 239 (Ind., Mar. 15, 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Miller Citizens Corp. v. Carter, 126 S. Ct. 398, 163 L. Ed. 2d 276, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 7190 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Lake County Superior Court, No. 45D05-0404-PL-91 The Honorable Robert A. Pete, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary injunction vacated; case remanded with instructions to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee taxpayers brought suit in the Lake County Superior Court (Indiana), for a declaratory judgment that two property tax reassessment statutes were unconstitutional and for an injunction. The trial court found the statutes violated the Indiana Constitution and granted a preliminary injunction. The Indiana Attorney General filed a petition for writ of mandamus and prohibition. The preliminary injunction was also appealed, but stayed.


OVERVIEW: The Indiana General Assembly passed Ind. Code § 6-1.1-4-32 (2004) and Ind. Code § 6-1.1-8.5-1 et seq., which applied only in Lake County of Indiana and provided for countywide reassessment of property for tax purposes to be conducted by the Indiana Department of Local Government Finance (DLGF) and by private contractors the DLGF selected. The Attorney General contended that exclusive jurisdiction over the case lied in the Indiana Tax Court. The Supreme Court of Indiana found that the lower court had no jurisdiction to hear the claims. The court further found that, although the laws violated Ind. Const. art. IV, § 22 as special legislation providing for the assessment of taxes, Ind. Code § 6-1.1-4-35 (2004), curative legislation the General Assembly later passed, validated the acts taken under the unconstitutional special legislation. Moreover, the taxpayers waited until reassessment was completed to seek injunctive relief. In the meantime, other taxpayers and local government units relied on the ongoing reassessment process to supply funding for essential government functions. Therefore, the taxpayers' claim for injunction was also barred by the delay in seeking equitable relief.


OUTCOME: The preliminary injunction entered by the trial court was vacated. The case was remanded with instructions to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction.

Corcoran v. State


NO. 02S00-0304-PD-00143


SUPREME COURT OF INDIANA


820 N.E.2d 655; 2005 Ind. LEXIS 10



January 11, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing granted by Corcoran v. State, 2005 Ind. LEXIS 448 (Ind., May 12, 2005)
Adhered to Corcoran v. State, 827 N.E.2d 542, 2005 Ind. LEXIS 447 (Ind., May 12, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Allen Superior Court, No. 02D04-9707-CF-465. The Honorable Frances C. Gull, Judge. On Petition To Transfer from the Allen County Superior Court, No. 02S00-0304-PD-143.
Corcoran v. State, 739 N.E.2d 649, 2000 Ind. LEXIS 1142 (Ind., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Corcoran's request for dismissal denied; trial court's determination of competency affirmed..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state public defender (PD) challenged a finding by the Allen Superior Court (Indiana), which found that defendant made a competent waiver of any further legal review of his convictions for four murders and his sentence of death. Defendant moved for dismissal of the appeal in order to allow him to pursue post-conviction relief (PCR).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's death sentence was affirmed on direct appeal, but he refused to sign a PCR petition, so the PD requested a competency hearing. The trial court found that defendant was competent to waive further legal review. The PD appealed, arguing that defendant's mental illness precluded the competency determination. The PD also claimed that the court was constitutionally and statutorily required to review the conviction and sentence and that executing a mentally ill person was unconstitutional. Defendant subsequently recanted his waiver. The State challenged the PD's standing to raise any issues other than a challenge to the competency finding. The court found that defendant did not file a PCR petition, the time to do so under Ind. R. Crim. P. 24(G)(2) had passed, and the issues on appeal were best decided promptly, so dismissal was denied. The evidence supported a conclusion that defendant could understand his legal position and was rational when he waived review, so the proper competency standard was applied. Moreover, the PD lacked standing to raise the other issues without defendant's consent. Finally, the court refused to extend automatic PCR to all death sentences.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding that defendant was competent to waive further collateral review. The court found that the PD did not have standing to raise other claims that were presented without defendant's consent and the court refused to adopt a policy that would extend automatic PCR to all death sentences. Finally, the court denied defendant's request to dismiss the appeal.
